<p><b>Islam</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="/Éª/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">Éª</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span href="#cite_note-1">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ù±ÙÙØ¥ÙØ³ÙÙÙØ§Ù</span>&#8206;, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">romanized</a>:&#160;</small><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">al-IslÄm</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[alÊÉªsËlaËm]</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ar-al_islam.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> teaching that the Arab religious leader <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">messenger</a> of God (<a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OEIW-allah_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEIW-allah-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> It is the <a href="/wiki/Largest_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Largest religion">world's second-largest religion</a> with over 1.9&#160;billion followers or 24.4% of the world's population,<sup id="cite_ref-pew2015_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew2015-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Muslims make up a majority of the population in <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">50 countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-landscape_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-landscape-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Islam teaches that <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> is <a href="/wiki/Mercy#Islam" title="Mercy">merciful</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">all-powerful</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">unique</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and has guided mankind through <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">prophets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_holy_books" title="Islamic holy books">revealed scriptures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayah">natural signs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OEIW-allah_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEIW-allah-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The primary scriptures of Islam are the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, believed to be the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/verbatim" class="extiw" title="wikt:verbatim">verbatim</a> word of God, and the teachings and normative examples (called the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a></i>, composed of accounts called <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i>) of Muhammad (<a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr></a> 570 â 8 June 632&#160;CE).<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Muslims believe that Islam is the complete and universal version of a <a href="/wiki/Fitra" title="Fitra">primordial faith</a> that was revealed many times before through prophets including <a href="/wiki/Adam_in_Islam" title="Adam in Islam">Adam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_in_Islam" title="Abraham in Islam">Abraham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Moses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-People-of-the-Book_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-People-of-the-Book-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> in its <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> to be the unaltered and final revelation of God.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Like other <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic religions</a>, Islam also teaches a final judgment with the righteous rewarded in <a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">paradise</a> and unrighteous punished in <a href="/wiki/Jahannam" title="Jahannam">hell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Religious concepts and practices include the <a href="/wiki/Five_Pillars_of_Islam" title="Five Pillars of Islam">Five Pillars of Islam</a>, which are obligatory acts of worship, and following Islamic law (<i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a></i>), which touches on virtually every aspect of life and society, from <a href="/wiki/Islamic_banking_and_finance" title="Islamic banking and finance">banking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">welfare</a> to <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Islam" title="Women in Islam">women</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_ethics#Environmentalism" title="Islamic ethics">environment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oxforddic_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxforddic-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The cities of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> are home to <a href="/wiki/Holiest_sites_in_Islam" title="Holiest sites in Islam">the three holiest sites in Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Trofimov_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trofimov-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aside from the <a href="/wiki/Aqidah" title="Aqidah">theological</a> narrative,<sup id="cite_ref-Esposito1998_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito1998-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peters2003_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peters2003-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Islam is <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_early_Islam" title="Historiography of early Islam">historically</a> believed to have originated in the early 7th century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a> in Mecca,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and by the 8th century the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> extended from <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Iberia</a> in the west to the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a> in the east. The <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> refers to the period traditionally dated from the 8th century to the 13th century, during the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>, when much of the historically <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> was experiencing a <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">scientific</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islamic_economics#Classical_Muslim_commerce" title="History of Islamic economics">economic</a> and cultural flourishing.<sup id="cite_ref-Saliba_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saliba-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-King_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hassan-Decline_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan-Decline-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The expansion of the Muslim world involved various caliphates and empires such as the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, trade and <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Conversion to Islam">conversion to Islam</a> by <a href="/wiki/Islamic_missionary_activity" title="Islamic missionary activity">missionary activities</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Dawah" title="Dawah">dawah</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Muslims are of one of two <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches" title="Islamic schools and branches">denominations</a>; <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> (75â90%)<sup id="cite_ref-Sunni-eb_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunni-eb-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a> (10-20%).<sup id="cite_ref-Shia_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shia-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> About 13% of Muslims live in <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Indonesia" title="Islam in Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, the largest Muslim-majority country;<sup id="cite_ref-pew2015countries_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew2015countries-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> 31% of Muslims live in <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_South_Asia" title="Islam in South Asia">South Asia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> the largest population of Muslims in the world;<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> 20% in the <a href="/wiki/MENA#Religion" title="MENA">Middle EastâNorth Africa</a> region,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> where it is the dominant religion;<sup id="cite_ref-pewmuslim22_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewmuslim22-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and 15% in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa#Religion" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pewmuslim32_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewmuslim32-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Sizeable <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">Muslim communities</a> are also found in the <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_Americas" title="Islam in the Americas">Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caucasus#Demographics" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Central_Asia" title="Islam in Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_China" title="Islam in China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Europe" title="Islam in Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Southeast_Asia#Islam" title="Mainland Southeast Asia">Mainland Southeast Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_Philippines" title="Islam in the Philippines">Philippines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Russia" title="Islam in Russia">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pewmuslim12_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pewmuslim12-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Islam is the <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_religion" title="Growth of religion">fastest-growing major religion in the world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USNewsLippman_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USNewsLippman-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_meaning">Etymology and meaning</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaaba_Mirror_like.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Kaaba_Mirror_like.jpg/250px-Kaaba_Mirror_like.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Kaaba_Mirror_like.jpg/375px-Kaaba_Mirror_like.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Kaaba_Mirror_like.jpg/500px-Kaaba_Mirror_like.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1632" data-file-height="1224" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaaba_Mirror_like.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Qibla" title="Qibla">direction of prayer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> destination of pilgrimage</div></div></div>
<p><i>IslÄm</i> (Arabic: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø¥Ø³ÙØ§Ù</span>&#8206;) is a verbal noun originating from the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">triliteral root</a> <a href="/wiki/S-L-M" class="mw-redirect" title="S-L-M">S-L-M</a> which forms a large class of words mostly relating to concepts of wholeness, submission, sincerity, safeness, and peace.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In a religious context, it means "voluntary submission to God".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D8%A5%D8%B3%D9%84%D8%A7%D9%85#Arabic" class="extiw" title="wikt:Ø¥Ø³ÙØ§Ù">IslÄm</a></i> is the verbal noun of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D8%A3%D8%B3%D9%84%D9%85#Arabic" class="extiw" title="wikt:Ø£Ø³ÙÙ">Form IV</a> of the root, and means "submission to God" or "surrender to God". <i><a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a></i>, the word applied to an adherent of Islam, is the <a href="/wiki/Active_participle" class="mw-redirect" title="Active participle">active participle</a> of the same verb form, and means "submitter to God" or "one who surrenders to God". The word sometimes has distinct connotations in its various occurrences in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>. In some verses, there is stress on the quality of Islam as an internal spiritual state: "Whomsoever God desires to guide, He opens his heart to Islam."<sup id="cite_ref-MULTIPLE-REFERENCES_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MULTIPLE-REFERENCES-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Other verses connect Islam and religion (<i><a href="/wiki/D%C4%ABn" class="mw-redirect" title="DÄ«n">dÄ«n</a></i>) together: "Today, I have perfected your religion (<i>dÄ«n</i>) for you; I have completed My blessing upon you; I have approved Islam for your religion."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Still others describe Islam as an action of returning to Godâmore than just a verbal affirmation of faith.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Hadith_of_Gabriel" title="Hadith of Gabriel">Hadith of Gabriel</a>, <i>islÄm</i> is presented as one part of a triad that also includes <i><a href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)" title="Iman (concept)">imÄn</a></i> (faith), and <i><a href="/wiki/Ihsan" title="Ihsan">ihsÄn</a></i> (excellence).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Islam was historically called <a href="/wiki/Mohammedan" title="Mohammedan">Muhammadanism</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anglophone" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglophone">Anglophone</a> societies. This term has fallen out of use and is sometimes said to be <a href="/wiki/Religious_offense" title="Religious offense">offensive</a> because it suggests that a human being rather than God is central to Muslims' religion, parallel to <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a> in <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Some authors, however, continue to use the term <i>Muhammadanism</i> as a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/technical_term" class="extiw" title="wikt:technical term">technical term</a> for the religious system as opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Theological" class="mw-redirect" title="Theological">theological</a> concept of Islam that exists within that system.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Articles_of_faith">Articles of faith</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Aqidah" title="Aqidah">Aqidah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)" title="Iman (concept)">Iman</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">Faith</a> (<a href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)" title="Iman (concept)">Iman</a>) in the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creed</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aqidah" title="Aqidah">Aqidah</a>) is often represented as the <a href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)#The_six_articles_of_the_Islamic_faith" title="Iman (concept)">six articles of faith</a>, notably spelled out in the <a href="/wiki/Hadith_of_Gabriel" title="Hadith of Gabriel">Hadith of Gabriel</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept_of_God">Concept of God</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God in Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Istanbul,_Hagia_Sophia,_Allah.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg/220px-Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg/330px-Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg/440px-Istanbul%2C_Hagia_Sophia%2C_Allah.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2118" data-file-height="2077" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Istanbul,_Hagia_Sophia,_Allah.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Medallion" class="mw-redirect" title="Medallion">Medallion</a> showing "<a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>" (God) in <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a></div></div></div>
<p>Islam is often seen as having the simplest doctrines of the major religions.<sup id="cite_ref-USNewsLippman_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USNewsLippman-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Its most fundamental concept is a rigorous monotheism, called <a href="/wiki/Taw%E1%B8%A5%C4%ABd" class="mw-redirect" title="Tawá¸¥Ä«d">tawá¸¥Ä«d</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ØªÙØ­ÙØ¯</span>&#8206;). God is described in chapter 112 of the Quran as: "Say, He is God, the One and Only; God, the Eternal, Absolute; He begetteth not, nor is He begotten; And there is none like unto Him" (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D112%3Averse%3D1â4">112:1â4</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Islam rejects <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatry</a>, called <i><a href="/wiki/Shirk_(Islam)" title="Shirk (Islam)">Shirk</a></i>, and reject the Christian doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>. In Islam, God is beyond all comprehension and thus Muslims are not expected to anthropomorphise him.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> God is described and referred to by certain names or attributes, the most common being <i>Al-RahmÄn</i>, meaning "The Compassionate" and <i>Al-RahÄ«m</i>, meaning "The Merciful" (See <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">Names of God in Islam</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Ben_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ben-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Islam teaches that the creation of everything in the universe was brought into being by God's command as expressed by the wording, "<a href="/wiki/Be,_and_it_is" title="Be, and it is">Be, and it is</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> and that the <a href="/wiki/Purpose_of_life" class="mw-redirect" title="Purpose of life">purpose of existence</a> is to worship or to <i>know</i> God.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> He is viewed as a personal god who responds whenever a person in need or distress calls him.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> There are no intermediaries, such as clergy, to contact God who states, "I am nearer to him than (his) <a href="/wiki/Jugular_vein" title="Jugular vein">jugular vein</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> God consciousness is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Taqwa" title="Taqwa">Taqwa</a>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/All%C4%81h" class="mw-redirect" title="AllÄh">AllÄh</a></i> is a term, with no <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a> being ascribed, used by Muslims and Arabic-speaking Christians and Jews in reference to God, while <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="ISO 233 Arabic (Arabic language) transliteration"><a href="/wiki/%CA%BEil%C4%81h" class="mw-redirect" title="Ê¾ilÄh">Ê¾ilÄh</a></i></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø¥ÙÙ</span>&#8206;) is a term used for a deity or a god in general.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Other non-Arab Muslims might use different names as much as Allah, for instance "TanrÄ±" in <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> "KhodÄ" in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> or "á¸´áºudÄ" in <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Angels">Angels</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Angels_in_Islam" title="Angels in Islam">Angels in Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mohammed_receiving_revelation_from_the_angel_Gabriel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Mohammed_receiving_revelation_from_the_angel_Gabriel.jpg/220px-Mohammed_receiving_revelation_from_the_angel_Gabriel.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Mohammed_receiving_revelation_from_the_angel_Gabriel.jpg/330px-Mohammed_receiving_revelation_from_the_angel_Gabriel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Mohammed_receiving_revelation_from_the_angel_Gabriel.jpg/440px-Mohammed_receiving_revelation_from_the_angel_Gabriel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="542" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mohammed_receiving_revelation_from_the_angel_Gabriel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muhammad receiving his first revelation from the angel <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a>. From the manuscript <a href="/wiki/Jami%27_al-tawarikh" title="Jami&#39; al-tawarikh">Jami' al-tawarikh</a> by <a href="/wiki/Rashid-al-Din_Hamadani" title="Rashid-al-Din Hamadani">Rashid-al-Din Hamadani</a>, 1307, <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> period.</div></div></div>
<p>Belief in <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angels</a> is fundamental to Islam. The Quranic word for angel (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ÙÙÙ</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration">malak</i></i>) derives either from <i>Malaka</i>, meaning "he controlled", due to their power to govern different affairs assigned to them,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> or from the root either from  <i>â-l-k</i>, <i>l-â-k</i> or <i>m-l-k</i> with the broad meaning of a "<a href="/wiki/Courier" title="Courier">messenger</a>", just like its counterparts in <a href="/wiki/Hebrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> (<i>malÊ¾Ã¡kh</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> (<i>angelos</i>). Unlike their Hebrew counterpart, the term is exclusively used for heavenly spirits of the divine world, but not for human messengers. The Quran refers to both angelic and human messengers as "rasul" instead.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Quran is the principal source for the Islamic concept of angels.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Some of them, such as <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_(archangel)" title="Michael (archangel)">Michael</a>, are mentioned by name in the Quran, others are only referred to by their function. In <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> literature, angels are often assigned to only one specific phenomena.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Angels play a significant role in <a href="/wiki/Isra_and_Mi%27raj" title="Isra and Mi&#39;raj">Mi'raj literature</a>, where Muhammad encounters several angels during his journey through the heavens.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Further angels have often been featured in Islamic eschatology, <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Islamic theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Duties assigned to angels include, for example, communicating <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelations</a> from God, glorifying God, recording every person's actions, and taking a person's <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a> at the time of death.
</p><p>In Islam, just like in Judaism and Christianity, angels are often represented in <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphic forms</a> combined with <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> images, such as wings, being of great size or wearing heavenly articles.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The Quran describes them as "messengers with wingsâtwo, or three, or four (pairs)..."<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Common characteristics for angels are their missing needs for bodily desires, such as eating and drinking.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Their lack of affinity to material desires is also expressed by their creation from light: Angels of mercy are created from <i>nur</i> (cold light) in opposition to the angels of punishment created from <i>nar</i> (hot light).<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Muslims do not generally share the perceptions of angelic pictorial depictions, such as those found in Western art.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revelations">Revelations</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wahy" title="Wahy">Wahy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_holy_books" title="Islamic holy books">Islamic holy books</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Quran" title="History of the Quran">History of the Quran</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FirstSurahKoran_(fragment).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg/220px-FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg/330px-FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg/440px-FirstSurahKoran_%28fragment%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="444" data-file-height="509" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FirstSurahKoran_(fragment).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first <a href="/wiki/Sura" class="mw-redirect" title="Sura">chapter</a> of the Quran, <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Fatiha" title="Al-Fatiha">Al-Fatiha</a></i> (<i>The Opening</i>), is seven verses</div></div></div>
<p>The Islamic holy books are the records which most Muslims believe were dictated by God to various prophets. <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> believe that parts of the previously revealed scriptures, the <i><a href="/wiki/Tawrat" class="mw-redirect" title="Tawrat">Tawrat</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Injil" class="mw-redirect" title="Injil">Injil</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Gospel_in_Islam" title="Gospel in Islam">Gospel</a>), had become <a href="/wiki/Tahrif" title="Tahrif">distorted</a>âeither in interpretation, in text, or both.<sup id="cite_ref-Distorted_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Distorted-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> (literally, "Recitation") is viewed by Muslims as the final revelation and literal word of God and is widely regarded as the finest <a href="/wiki/Arabic_literature" title="Arabic literature">literary</a> work in the classical <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Muslims believe that the verses of the Quran were revealed to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> by God through the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Spirit (Islam)">archangel Gabriel</a> (<i>JibrÄ«l</i>) on many occasions between 610 CE until his death on June 8, 632.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> While Muhammad was alive, all of these revelations were written down by his companions (<i><a href="/wiki/Sahabah" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahabah">sahabah</a></i>), although the prime method of transmission was orally through <a href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Quran)" title="Hafiz (Quran)">memorization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-al_faruqi_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-al_faruqi-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Quran is divided into 114 chapters (<i><a href="/wiki/Sura" class="mw-redirect" title="Sura">suras</a></i>) which combined, contain 6,236 verses (<i><a href="/wiki/Ayah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayah">ÄyÄt</a></i>). The chronologically earlier suras, revealed at <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, are primarily concerned with ethical and spiritual topics. The later <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medinan</a> suras mostly discuss social and legal issues relevant to the Muslim community.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Quran is more concerned with moral guidance than legislation, and is considered the "sourcebook of Islamic principles and values".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Muslim jurists consult the <i>hadith</i> ("reports"), or the written record of Prophet Muhammad's life, to both supplement the Quran and assist with its interpretation. The science of Quranic commentary and exegesis is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">tafsir</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The set of rules governing proper <a href="/wiki/Elocution" title="Elocution">elocution</a> of recitation is called <i><a href="/wiki/Tajwid" title="Tajwid">tajwid</a></i>.
</p><p>Muslims usually view "the Quran" as the original scripture as revealed in Arabic and that any translations are necessarily deficient, which are regarded only as commentaries on the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prophets_and_sunnah">Prophets and sunnah</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">Prophets and messengers in Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">Sunnah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Persian_manuscript_Muhammad_leads_Abraham_Moses_Jesus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Medieval_Persian_manuscript_Muhammad_leads_Abraham_Moses_Jesus.jpg/220px-Medieval_Persian_manuscript_Muhammad_leads_Abraham_Moses_Jesus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Medieval_Persian_manuscript_Muhammad_leads_Abraham_Moses_Jesus.jpg/330px-Medieval_Persian_manuscript_Muhammad_leads_Abraham_Moses_Jesus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Medieval_Persian_manuscript_Muhammad_leads_Abraham_Moses_Jesus.jpg/440px-Medieval_Persian_manuscript_Muhammad_leads_Abraham_Moses_Jesus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1073" data-file-height="680" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Persian_manuscript_Muhammad_leads_Abraham_Moses_Jesus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Persian miniature depicts Muhammad leading <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> and other prophets in prayer.</div></div></div>
<p>Muslims identify the 'prophets' (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø£ÙØ¨ÙØ§Ø¡</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration">anbiyÄÊ¾</i></i> ) of Islam as those humans chosen by God to be his messengers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="If this is the definition of a prophet, how can Adam be considered a prophet? Isn&#39;t it rather that prophets can talk to God?. A citation can be helpful to find a appropriate definition. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> According to the Quran, the prophets were instructed by God to bring the "will of God" to the peoples of the nations. Muslims believe that prophets are human and not divine, though some are able to perform miracles to prove their claim. <a href="/wiki/Schools_of_Islamic_theology" title="Schools of Islamic theology">Islamic theology</a> says that all of God's messengers preached the message of Islamâsubmission to the will of God. The Quran mentions the names of numerous figures considered <a href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophets in Islam">prophets in Islam</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noah_in_Islam" title="Noah in Islam">Noah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Moses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus</a>, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Muslims believe that God finally sent Muhammad as the last law-bearing prophet (<i><a href="/wiki/Seal_of_the_prophets" class="mw-redirect" title="Seal of the prophets">Seal of the prophets</a></i>) to convey the divine message to the whole world (to sum up and to finalize the word of God). In Islam, the "normative" example of Muhammad's life is called the <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a> (literally "trodden path"). Muslims are encouraged to emulate Muhammad's actions in their daily lives and the sunnah is seen as crucial to guiding interpretation of the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> This example is preserved in traditions known as hadith, which recount his words, his actions, and his personal characteristics. Hadith Qudsi is a sub-category of hadith, regarded as verbatim words of God quoted by Muhammad but is not part of the Quran.
</p><p>A hadith involves two elements: a chain of narrators, called <a href="/wiki/Isnad" class="mw-redirect" title="Isnad">sanad</a>, and the actual wording, called <a href="/wiki/Matn" class="mw-redirect" title="Matn">matn</a>. Hadiths can be classified, by studying the narration, as "authentic" or "correct", called <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith_terminology#á¹¢aá¸¥Ä«á¸¥" title="Hadith terminology">sahih</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ØµÙØ­ÙÙÙØ­</span>&#8206;), "good", called <i><a href="/wiki/Hasan_(hadith)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hasan (hadith)">hasan</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø­ÙØ³ÙÙ</span>&#8206;) or "weak", called <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith_terminology#á¸aÊ»Ä«f" title="Hadith terminology">daÊ»Ä«f</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø¶ÙØ¹ÙÙÙÙ</span>&#8206;) among others. <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Bukhari" title="Muhammad al-Bukhari">Muhammad al-Bukhari</a><sup id="cite_ref-sahih-bukhari.com_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sahih-bukhari.com-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> collected over 300,000 hadith, but only included 2,602 distinct hadith that passed veracity tests that codified them as authentic into his book <a href="/wiki/Sahih_al-Bukhari" title="Sahih al-Bukhari">Sahih al-Bukhari</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-sahih-bukhari.com_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sahih-bukhari.com-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> which is considered by Sunnis to be the most <a href="/wiki/Hadith_terminology#Terminology_relating_to_the_authenticity_of_a_hadith" title="Hadith terminology">authentic</a> source after the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Muqaddimah_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Muqaddimah-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Another famous source(s) of hadiths is known as <a href="/wiki/The_Four_Books" title="The Four Books">The Four Books</a>, which Shias consider as the most authentic hadith reference.<sup id="cite_ref-Meri_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meri-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resurrection_and_judgment">Resurrection and judgment</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">Islamic eschatology</a></div>
<p>Belief in the "Day of Resurrection", <i><a href="/wiki/Qiyamah" title="Qiyamah">Yawm al-QiyÄmah</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ÙÙÙ Ø§ÙÙÙØ§ÙØ©</span>&#8206;) is also crucial for Muslims. They believe the time of <i>QiyÄmah</i> is preordained by God but unknown to man. The trials and <a href="/wiki/Tribulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Tribulation">tribulations</a> preceding and during the <i>QiyÄmah</i> are described in the Quran and the hadith, and also in the commentaries of <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">scholars</a>. The Quran emphasizes <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_Dead" class="mw-redirect" title="Resurrection of the Dead">bodily resurrection</a>, a break from the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Pre-Islamic Arabia">pre-Islamic Arabian</a> understanding of death.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On Yawm al-QiyÄmah, Muslims believe all humankind will be judged on their good and bad deeds and consigned to <i><a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">Jannah</a></i> (paradise) or <i><a href="/wiki/Jahannam" title="Jahannam">Jahannam</a></i> (hell). The QurÊ¼an in Surat al-Zalzalah describes this as, "So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it (99:7) and whoever does an atom's weight of evil will see it (99:8)." The QurÊ¼an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_of_sin" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic views of sin">lists several sins</a> that can condemn a person to <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">hell</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Kafir" title="Kafir">disbelief</a> in God (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ÙÙØ±</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration">kufr</i></i>), and dishonesty; however, the QurÊ¼an makes it clear God will forgive the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_sin" title="Islamic views on sin">sins</a> of those who repent if he so wills. Good deeds, such as charity, prayer and compassion towards animals,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> will be rewarded with entry to heaven. Muslims view <a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">heaven</a> as a place of joy and blessings, with QurÊ¼anic references describing its features. Mystical traditions in Islam place these heavenly delights in the context of an ecstatic awareness of God.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> <i>Yawm al-QiyÄmah</i> is also identified in the Quran as <i>Yawm ad-DÄ«n</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ÙÙÙ Ø§ÙØ¯ÙÙ</span>&#8206;), "Day of Religion";<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <i>as-sÄÊ¿ah</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø§ÙØ³Ø§Ø¹Ø©</span>&#8206;), "the Last Hour";<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> and <i>al-QÄriÊ¿ah</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø§ÙÙØ§Ø±Ø¹Ø©</span>&#8206;), "The Clatterer".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divine_will">Divine will</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Predestination_in_Islam" title="Predestination in Islam">Predestination in Islam</a></div>
<p>The concept of divine will is referred to as <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="DIN 31635 Arabic (Arabic language) transliteration">al-qadÄÊ¾ wa l-qadar</i></i>, which literally derives from a root that means <i>to measure</i>. Everything, good and bad, is believed to have been decreed.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Acts_of_worship">Acts of worship</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Five_Pillars_of_Islam" title="Five Pillars of Islam">Five Pillars of Islam</a></div>
<p>There are five basic religious acts in Islam, collectively known as 'The Pillars of Islam' (<i>arkan al-Islam</i>; also <i>arkan ad-din</i>, "pillars of religion"), which are considered obligatory for all believers. The Quran presents them as a framework for worship and a sign of commitment to the faith. They are (1) the creed (<a href="/wiki/Shahada" title="Shahada">Shahada</a>), (2) daily prayers (<a href="/wiki/Salah" title="Salah">Salah</a>), (3) almsgiving (<a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">Zakat</a>), (4) fasting during <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Fasting_in_Islam" title="Fasting in Islam">Sawm</a>) and (5) the pilgrimage to Mecca (<a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a>) at least once in a lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> sects agree on the essential details for the performance of these acts.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Apart from these, Muslims also perform other religious acts. Notable among them are charity (<a href="/wiki/Sadaqah" title="Sadaqah">Sadaqah</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Quran_reading" class="mw-redirect" title="Quran reading">recitation of the Quran</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Testimony">Testimony</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg/220px-Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg/330px-Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg/440px-Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="588" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_Rupee_Akbar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver coin of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal Emperor">Mughal Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Akbar_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Akbar the Great">Akbar the Great</a>, inscribed with the <i>Shahadah</i></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shahadah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shahadah">Shahadah</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Shahadah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shahadah">Shahadah</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> which is the basic <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creed</a> of Islam that must be recited under <a href="/wiki/Oath" title="Oath">oath</a> with the specific statement: "<i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="DIN 31635 Arabic (Arabic language) transliteration">Ê¾aÅ¡hadu Ê¾al-lÄ Ê¾ilÄha Ê¾illÄ-llÄhu wa Ê¾aÅ¡hadu Ê¾anna muÄ§ammadan rasÅ«lu-llÄh</i></i>", or "I testify that there is no god but <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>, Muhammad is the messenger of God"<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> (<span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">Ø£Ø´ÙØ¯ Ø£Ù ÙØ§ Ø¥ÙÙ Ø¥ÙØ§ Ø§ÙÙÙ ÙØ£Ù ÙØ­ÙØ¯Ø§ Ø±Ø³ÙÙ Ø§ÙÙÙ</span>). This testament is a foundation for all other beliefs and practices in Islam. Muslims must repeat the <i>shahadah</i> in prayer, and non-Muslims wishing to <a href="/wiki/Convert_to_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Convert to Islam">convert to Islam</a> are required to recite the creed.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prayer">Prayer</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Salat" class="mw-redirect" title="Salat">Salat</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">Mosque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jumu%27ah" title="Jumu&#39;ah">Jumu'ah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mosque.jpg/220px-Mosque.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mosque.jpg/330px-Mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mosque.jpg/440px-Mosque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mosque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muslim men <a href="/wiki/Prostration" title="Prostration">prostrating</a> in prayer, at the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Mosque" title="Umayyad Mosque">Umayyad Mosque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Ritual prayers are called á¹¢alÄh or á¹¢alÄt (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D8%B5%D9%84%D8%A7%D8%A9" class="extiw" title="wikt:ØµÙØ§Ø©">ØµÙØ§Ø©</a>). Salat is intended to focus the mind on God, and is seen as a personal communication with him that expresses gratitude and <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a>. Performing prayers five times a day is compulsory but flexibility in the timing specifics is allowed depending on circumstances. The prayers are recited in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a>, and consist of verses from the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The prayers are done with the chest in direction of the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">kaaba</a> though in the early days of Islam, they were done in direction of <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>. The act of supplicating is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Dua" title="Dua">dua</a>.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">Mosque</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Places_of_worship" class="mw-redirect" title="Places of worship">place of worship</a> for Muslims, who often refer to it by its Arabic name <i>masjid</i>. A large mosque for gathering for Friday prayers or Eid prayers are called <a href="/wiki/Jama_Masjid" class="mw-redirect" title="Jama Masjid">masjid jÄmi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Although the primary purpose of the mosque is to serve as a place of prayer, it is also important to the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Muslim community</a> as a place to meet and study. In Medina, <a href="/wiki/Al-Masjid_al-Nabawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Masjid al-Nabawi">Al-Masjid al-Nabawi</a>, or the Prophet's Mosque, was also a place of refuge for the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Modern mosques have evolved greatly from the early designs of the 7th century, and contain a variety of architectural elements such as <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minarets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The means used to signal the approach of prayer time is a vocal call, known as the <a href="/wiki/Adhan" title="Adhan">adhan</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Charity">Charity</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">Zakat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sadaqah" title="Sadaqah">Sadaqah</a></div>
<p>"ZakÄt" (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø²ÙØ§Ø©</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration">zakÄh</i></i> "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/alms" class="extiw" title="wikt:alms">alms</a>") is giving a fixed portion (2.5%  annually)<sup id="cite_ref-AhmedGianci_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AhmedGianci-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Financial_capital" title="Financial capital">accumulated wealth</a> by those who can afford it to help the poor or needy, such as for freeing captives or those in <a href="/wiki/Bonded_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Bonded labour">debt</a> or (stranded) travellers, and for those employed to collect Zakat.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> It is considered a religious obligation (as opposed to supererogatory charity) that the well-off owe to the needy because their wealth is seen as a "trust from God's bounty". Conservative estimates of annual zakat is estimated to be 15 times global humanitarian aid contributions.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">Caliph</a> <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> distributed Zakat as one of the first examples of a <a href="/wiki/Guaranteed_minimum_income" title="Guaranteed minimum income">guaranteed minimum income</a>, with each man, woman and child getting 10 to 20 <a href="/wiki/Dirham" title="Dirham">dirhams</a> annually.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sadaqah" title="Sadaqah">Sadaqah</a> means optional charity which is practiced as religious duty and out of generosity.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Both the Quran and the hadith have put much emphasis on spending money for the welfare of needy people,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> and have urged the Muslims to give more as an act of optional charity.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The Quran says: "Spend something (in charity) out of the substance which We have bestowed on you, before Death should come to any of you" (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D63%3Averse%3D10">63:10</a>). One of the early teachings of Muhammad was that <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> expects men to be generous with their wealth and not to be miserly (Quran <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D107%3Averse%3D1">107:1â7</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Accumulating wealth without spending it to address the needs of the poor is generally prohibited and admonished.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Another kind of charity in Islam is <a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">waqf</a> which means perpetual religious endowment.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fasting">Fasting</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iftar_for_Ramadhan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Iftar_for_Ramadhan.jpg/220px-Iftar_for_Ramadhan.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="115" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Iftar_for_Ramadhan.jpg/330px-Iftar_for_Ramadhan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Iftar_for_Ramadhan.jpg/440px-Iftar_for_Ramadhan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="1576" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iftar_for_Ramadhan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A fast-breaking feast, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Iftar" title="Iftar">Iftar</a></i>, is served traditionally with <a href="/wiki/Date_(fruits)" class="mw-redirect" title="Date (fruits)">dates</a></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fasting_in_Islam" title="Fasting in Islam">Fasting in Islam</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fasting_during_Ramadan" title="Fasting during Ramadan">Fasting during Ramadan</a></div>
<p>Fasting (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ØµÙÙ</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration">á¹£awm</i></i>) from food and drink, among other things, must be performed from dawn to dusk during the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>. The fast is to encourage a feeling of nearness to God, and during it Muslims should express their gratitude for and dependence on him, atone for their past sins, develop self-control and restraint and think of the needy. <i>Sawm</i> is not obligatory for several groups for whom it would constitute an undue burden. For others, flexibility is allowed depending on circumstances, but missed fasts must be compensated for later.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilgrimage">Pilgrimage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_packed_house_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/A_packed_house_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/220px-A_packed_house_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/A_packed_house_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/330px-A_packed_house_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/A_packed_house_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/440px-A_packed_house_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_packed_house_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pilgrims at the <a href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Haram" class="mw-redirect" title="Masjid al-Haram">Masjid al-Haram</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> during <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umrah" title="Umrah">Umrah</a></div>
<p>The obligatory Islamic <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a>, called the <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration">á¸¥ajj</i></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø­Ø¬</span>&#8206;), has to be performed during the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic month</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah" title="Dhu al-Hijjah">Dhu al-Hijjah</a></i> in the city of Mecca. Every able-bodied Muslim who can afford it must make the pilgrimage to Mecca at least once in his or her lifetime. Rituals of the Hajj include: spending a day and a night in the tents in the desert plain of Mina, then a day in the desert plain of Arafat praying and worshiping God, following the foot steps of Abraham; then spending a night out in the open, sleeping on the desert sand in the desert plain of Muzdalifah; then moving to Jamarat, symbolically <a href="/wiki/Stoning_of_the_Devil" title="Stoning of the Devil">stoning the Devil</a> recounting Abraham's actions;<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> then going to Mecca and walking seven times around the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> which Muslims believe was built as a place of worship by Abraham; then walking seven times between <a href="/wiki/Al-Safa_and_Al-Marwah" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Safa and Al-Marwah">Mount Safa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Safa_and_Al-Marwah" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Safa and Al-Marwah">Mount Marwah</a> recounting the steps of Abraham's wife, <a href="/wiki/Hagar" title="Hagar">Hagar</a>, while she was looking for water for her son <a href="/wiki/Ishmael_in_Islam" title="Ishmael in Islam">Ishmael</a> in the desert before Mecca developed into a settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Another form of pilgrimage, <a href="/wiki/Umrah" title="Umrah">Umrah</a>, can be undertaken at any time of the year.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quranic_recitation_and_memorisation">Quranic recitation and memorisation</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque,_Damascus,_Syria.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/220px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/330px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/440px-Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2813" data-file-height="1875" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Men_reading_the_Koran_in_Umayyad_Mosque,_Damascus,_Syria.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muslim men reading the Quran</div></div></div>
<p>Muslims recite and memorize the whole or part of the Quran as acts of virtue. Reciting the Quran with <a href="/wiki/Tajwid" title="Tajwid">elocution</a> has been described as an excellent act of worship.<sup id="cite_ref-Nigosian-70_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nigosian-70-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Pious Muslims recite the whole Quran at the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stefon-42_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stefon-42-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> In Islamic societies, any social program generally begins with the recitation of the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-Stefon-42_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stefon-42-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> One who has memorized the whole Quran is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Quran)" title="Hafiz (Quran)">hafiz</a></i> who, it is said, will be able to intercede for ten people on the Last Judgment Day.<sup id="cite_ref-Nigosian-70_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nigosian-70-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Apart from this, almost every Muslim memorizes some portion of the Quran because they need to recite it during their prayers.
</p>

</div></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
