<a href="/wiki/Islamic_studies" title="Islamic studies">Islamic studies</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Fiqh" title="Template:Fiqh"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Fiqh" title="Template talk:Fiqh"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Fiqh&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Fiqh</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Logic_in_Islamic_philosophy#Islamic_law_and_theology" title="Logic in Islamic philosophy">Logic in Islamic philosophy Â§&#160;Islamic law and theology</a></div>
<p>Sharia is the <a href="/wiki/Religious_law" title="Religious law">religious law</a> forming part of the Islamic tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-oxforddic_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxforddic-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> It is derived from the religious precepts of Islam, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, the term <i>sharÄ«Ê¿ah</i> refers to God's divine law and is contrasted with <i><a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a></i>, which refers to its scholarly interpretations.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vikor_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The manner of its application in modern times has been a subject of dispute between Muslim traditionalists and reformists.<sup id="cite_ref-oxforddic_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxforddic-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional <a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Islamic_jurisprudence" title="Principles of Islamic jurisprudence">theory of Islamic jurisprudence</a> recognizes four <a href="/wiki/Sources_of_sharia" title="Sources of sharia">sources of sharia</a>: the Quran, <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a></i> (Hadith and <a href="/wiki/Prophetic_biography" title="Prophetic biography">Sira</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">qiyas</a></i> (analogical reasoning), and <i><a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">ijma</a></i> (juridical consensus).<sup id="cite_ref-Esposito,_John_2001_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito,_John_2001-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Different <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">legal schools</a> developed methodologies for deriving sharia rulings from scriptural sources using a process known as <i><a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a></i> (inference).<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Traditional jurisprudence</a> distinguishes two principal branches of law, <i><a href="/wiki/Ibadah" title="Ibadah">Ê¿ibÄdÄt</a></i> (rituals) and <i><a href="/wiki/Muamalat" title="Muamalat">muÊ¿ÄmalÄt</a></i> (social relations), which together comprise a wide range of topics.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Its rulings assign actions to one of <a href="/wiki/Ahkam" title="Ahkam">five categories</a>: <a href="/wiki/Fard" title="Fard">mandatory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mustahabb" title="Mustahabb">recommended</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mubah" title="Mubah">permitted</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makruh" title="Makruh">abhorred</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Haram" title="Haram">prohibited</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_125-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vikor_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Thus, some areas of sharia overlap with the Western notion of law while others correspond more broadly to living life in accordance with God's will.<sup id="cite_ref-vikor_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, sharia was interpreted by independent jurists (<a href="/wiki/Mufti" title="Mufti">muftis</a>). Their legal opinions (<a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwas</a>) were taken into account by ruler-appointed <a href="/wiki/Qadi" title="Qadi">judges</a> who presided over <i><a href="/wiki/Q%C4%81%E1%B8%8D%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="QÄá¸Ä«">qÄá¸Ä«</a>'s</i> courts, and by <i>maáºÄlim</i> courts, which were controlled by the ruler's council and administered criminal law.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI_125-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vikor_126-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In the modern era, sharia-based criminal laws were widely replaced by statutes inspired by European models.<sup id="cite_ref-vikor_126-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>'s 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Tanzimat" title="Tanzimat">Tanzimat</a> reforms lead to the <a href="/wiki/Mecelle" title="Mecelle">Mecelle</a> civil code and represented the first attempt to <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codify</a> Sharia.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> While the constitutions of most Muslim-majority states contain references to sharia, its classical rules were largely retained only in <a href="/wiki/Status_(law)" title="Status (law)">personal status</a> (family) laws.<sup id="cite_ref-vikor_126-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Legislative bodies which codified these laws sought to modernize them without abandoning their foundations in traditional jurisprudence.<sup id="cite_ref-vikor_126-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mayer_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayer-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Islamic_revival" title="Islamic revival">Islamic revival</a> of the late 20th century brought along calls by <a href="/wiki/Islamist" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamist">Islamist</a> movements for full implementation of sharia.<sup id="cite_ref-vikor_126-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vikor-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mayer_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayer-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The role of sharia has become a contested topic around the world. There are ongoing debates as to whether sharia is compatible with secular forms of government, human rights, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_thought" title="Freedom of thought">freedom of thought</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women&#39;s rights">women's rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-naim96_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-naim96-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hajjar2004_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hajjar2004-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholars">Scholars</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:234px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9A%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%91%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8._%D0%A2%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%88%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/%D0%9A%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%91%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8._%D0%A2%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%88%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9_%28cropped%29.jpg/232px-%D0%9A%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%91%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8._%D0%A2%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%88%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="232" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/%D0%9A%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%91%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8._%D0%A2%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%88%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9_%28cropped%29.jpg/348px-%D0%9A%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%91%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8._%D0%A2%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%88%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/%D0%9A%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%91%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8._%D0%A2%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%88%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9_%28cropped%29.jpg/464px-%D0%9A%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%91%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8._%D0%A2%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%88%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5897" data-file-height="3939" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D0%9A%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BB%D0%BE_%D0%91%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8._%D0%A2%D0%B0%D1%82%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%88%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%BB%D1%8F_%D0%B4%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%B9_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Crimean_Tatars" title="Crimean Tatars">Crimean Tatar</a> Muslim students (1856)</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">Ulama</a></div>
<p>Islam, like Judaism, has no clergy in the <a href="/wiki/Sacerdotal" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacerdotal">sacerdotal</a> sense, such as priests who mediate between God and people. However, there are many terms in Islam to refer to religiously sanctioned positions of Islam. In the broadest sense, the term <i>ulema</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø¹ÙÙØ§Ø¡</span>&#8206;) is used to describe the body of Muslim scholars who have completed several years of training and study of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_studies" title="Islamic studies">Islamic sciences</a>. A jurist who interprets Islamic law is called a <a href="/wiki/Mufti" title="Mufti">mufti</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ÙÙØªÙ</span>&#8206;) and often issues legal opinions, called <a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwas</a>. A scholar of jurisprudence is called a <a href="/wiki/Faqih" class="mw-redirect" title="Faqih">faqih</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ÙÙÙÙ</span>&#8206;). Someone who studies the science of hadith is called a <a href="/wiki/Muhaddith" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhaddith">muhaddith</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Qadi" title="Qadi">qadi</a> is a judge in an Islamic court. <a href="/wiki/Honorific" title="Honorific">Honorific</a> titles given to scholars include <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">sheikh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mullah" title="Mullah">mullah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mawlawi_(Islamic_title)" title="Mawlawi (Islamic title)">mawlawi</a>. <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imam</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø¥ÙØ§Ù</span>&#8206;) is a leadership position, often used in the context of conducting Islamic worship services.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Schools_of_jurisprudence">Schools of jurisprudence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Madhhab_Map3.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Madhhab_Map3.png/260px-Madhhab_Map3.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Madhhab_Map3.png/390px-Madhhab_Map3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Madhhab_Map3.png/520px-Madhhab_Map3.png 2x" data-file-width="1245" data-file-height="604" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Madhhab_Map3.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Islamic <a href="/wiki/Madh%27hab" class="mw-redirect" title="Madh&#39;hab">schools of law</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">Madhhab</a></div>
<p>A school of jurisprudence is referred to as a <i>madhab</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ÙØ°ÙØ¨</span>&#8206;). The four major Sunni schools are the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Shafi&#39;i">Shafi'i</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a> and sometimes <a href="/wiki/%E1%BA%92%C4%81hir%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="áºÄhirÄ«">áºÄhirÄ«</a> while the three major Shia schools are <a href="/wiki/Ja%27fari" class="mw-redirect" title="Ja&#39;fari">Ja'fari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaidi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma&#39;ilism">Isma'ili</a>. Each differ in their methodology, called <a href="/wiki/Usul_al-fiqh" class="mw-redirect" title="Usul al-fiqh">Usul al-fiqh</a>. The following of decisions by a religious expert without necessarily examining the decision's reasoning is called <a href="/wiki/Taqlid" title="Taqlid">taqlid</a>. The term <i>ghair muqallid</i> literally refers to those who do not use taqlid and by extension do not have a <a href="/wiki/Madhab" class="mw-redirect" title="Madhab">madhab</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The practice of an individual interpretating law with independent reasoning is called <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_economics" title="Islamic economics">Islamic economics</a></div>
<p>To reduce the gap between the rich and the poor, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_economic_jurisprudence" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic economic jurisprudence">Islamic economic jurisprudence</a> encourages trade,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> discourages the hoarding of wealth and outlaws interest-bearing loans (<a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">usury</a>; the term is <i><a href="/wiki/Riba" title="Riba">riba</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, wealth is taxed through <a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">Zakat</a>, but trade is not taxed. <a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">Usury</a>, which allows the rich to get richer without sharing in the risk, is forbidden in Islam. Profit sharing and venture capital where the lender is also exposed to risk is acceptable.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Hoarding of food for speculation is also discouraged.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The taking of land belonging to others is also prohibited. The prohibition of <a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">usury</a> has resulted in the development of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_banking" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic banking">Islamic banking</a>. During the time of Muhammad, any money that went to the state, was immediately used to help the poor. Then in 634, <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a> formally established the welfare state <a href="/wiki/Bayt_al-mal" title="Bayt al-mal">Bayt al-mal</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bayt_al-mal" title="Bayt al-mal">Bayt al-mal</a> or the welfare state was for the Muslim and Non-Muslim poor, needy, elderly, orphans, widows, and the disabled. The <a href="/wiki/Bayt_al-mal" title="Bayt al-mal">Bayt al-mal</a> ran for hundreds of years under the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a> in the 7th century and continued through the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad" class="mw-redirect" title="Umayyad">Umayyad</a> period and well into the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a> era. Umar also introduced Child Benefit and Pensions for the children and the elderly.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Al-Buraey1985_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Al-Buraey1985-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jihad">Jihad</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">Jihad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_military_jurisprudence" title="Islamic military jurisprudence">Islamic military jurisprudence</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_expeditions_of_Muhammad" title="List of expeditions of Muhammad">List of expeditions of Muhammad</a></div>
<p>Jihad means "to strive or struggle" (in the way of God). Jihad, in its broadest sense, is "exerting one's utmost power, efforts, endeavors, or ability in contending with an object of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/disapprobation" class="extiw" title="wikt:disapprobation">disapprobation</a>". Depending on the object being a visible enemy, the <a href="/wiki/Devil" title="Devil">Devil</a>, and aspects of one's own self (such as sinful desires), different categories of jihad are defined.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Jihad also refers to one's striving to attain religious and moral perfection.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> When used without any qualifier, Jihad is understood in its military form.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>  Some Muslim authorities, especially among the Shi'a and <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufis</a>, distinguish between the "greater jihad", which pertains to spiritual <a href="/wiki/Self-improvement" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-improvement">self-perfection</a>, and the "lesser jihad", defined as warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Within <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Islamic jurisprudence</a>, jihad is usually taken to mean military exertion against non-Muslim combatants.<sup id="cite_ref-OEIP_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEIP-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EI2_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Jihad is the only form of warfare permissible in Islamic law and may be declared against illegal works, terrorists, criminal groups, rebels, <a href="/wiki/Apostasy_in_Islam" title="Apostasy in Islam">apostates</a>, and leaders or states who oppress Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jihad_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jihad-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Most Muslims today interpret Jihad as only a defensive form of warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Jihad only becomes an individual duty for those vested with authority. For the rest of the populace, this happens only in the case of a <a href="/wiki/General_mobilization" class="mw-redirect" title="General mobilization">general mobilization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jihad_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jihad-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> For most <a href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver Shias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Offensive_jihad" class="mw-redirect" title="Offensive jihad">offensive jihad</a> can only be declared by a <a href="/wiki/Imamah_(Shi%27a_twelver_doctrine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Imamah (Shi&#39;a twelver doctrine)">divinely appointed leader</a> of the Muslim community, and as such is suspended since <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Mahdi" title="Muhammad al-Mahdi">Muhammad al-Mahdi</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Occultation_(Islam)" title="Occultation (Islam)">occultation</a> in 868 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Islam_2003_pp_72_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Islam_2003_pp_72-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mysticism">Mysticism</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sufi%E2%80%93Salafi_relations" title="SufiâSalafi relations">SufiâSalafi relations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mevlana_Konya.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mevlana_Konya.jpg/220px-Mevlana_Konya.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mevlana_Konya.jpg/330px-Mevlana_Konya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mevlana_Konya.jpg/440px-Mevlana_Konya.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="375" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mevlana_Konya.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb of Sufi-mystic <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a>, Turkey</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a>, or <i>tasawwuf</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">ØªØµÙÙ</span>&#8206;), is a <a href="/wiki/Mystical" class="mw-redirect" title="Mystical">mystical</a>-<a href="/wiki/Ascetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascetic">ascetic</a> approach to Islam that seeks to find a direct <a href="/wiki/Divine_presence" title="Divine presence">personal experience of God</a>. It is not a sect of Islam and its adherents belong to the various Muslim denominations. Classical Sufi scholars defined <i>Tasawwuf</i> as "a science whose objective is the reparation of the heart and turning it away from all else but God", by means of "intuitive and emotional faculties" that one must be trained to use.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_Zarruq_2008_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed_Zarruq_2008-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_Zarruq_2008_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed_Zarruq_2008-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Sufis themselves claim that <i>Tasawwuf</i> is an aspect of Islam similar to <i>sharia</i>, inseparable from Islam and an integral part of Islamic belief and practice.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Religiousity of early Sufi ascetics, such as <a href="/wiki/Hasan_al-Basri" title="Hasan al-Basri">Hasan al-Basri</a>, emphazised fear to fail God's expectations of obedience, in contrast to later and more prominent Sufis, such as <a href="/wiki/Mansur_Al-Hallaj" title="Mansur Al-Hallaj">Mansur Al-Hallaj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jalaluddin_Rumi" class="mw-redirect" title="Jalaluddin Rumi">Jalaluddin Rumi</a>, whose religiousity is based on love towards God. For that reason, some academic scholars refuse to refer to the former as <i>Sufis</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, is Hasan al-Basri often portrayed as one of the earliest Sufis in Sufi traditions<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> and his ideas were later developed by the influential theologian <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Traditional Sufis, such as <a href="/wiki/Bayazid_Bastami" title="Bayazid Bastami">Bayazid Bastami</a>, Jalaluddin Rumi, <a href="/wiki/Haji_Bektash_Veli" title="Haji Bektash Veli">Haji Bektash Veli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Junaid_Baghdadi" class="mw-redirect" title="Junaid Baghdadi">Junaid Baghdadi</a>, and Al-Ghazali, argued for Sufism as being based upon the tenets of Islam and the teachings of the prophet.<sup id="cite_ref-chittick_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chittick-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasr_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nasr-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Sufis played an important role in the formation of Muslim societies through their missionary and educational activities.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Popular devotional practices such as veneration of <a href="/wiki/Wali" title="Wali">Sufi saints</a> have faced stiff opposition from followers of <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabism</a>, who have sometimes physically attacked Sufis leading to deterioration in <a href="/wiki/Sufi%E2%80%93Salafi_relations" title="SufiâSalafi relations">SufiâSalafi relations</a>. Sufism enjoyed a strong revival in Central Asia and South Asia; the <a href="/wiki/Barelvi" title="Barelvi">Barelvi</a> movement is Sufi influenced <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islam</a> with over 200 million followers,<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> largely in South Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-journals.cambridge.org_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-journals.cambridge.org-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>  Sufism is also prominent is Central Asia, where different orders are the main religious sources,<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> as well as in African countries such as <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mystical interpretations of Islam have also been developed by <a href="/wiki/Ismaili" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismaili">Ismaili</a> Shias, by the <a href="/wiki/Illuminationism" title="Illuminationism">School of Illumination</a>, as well as by the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Isfahan" title="School of Isfahan">Isfahan school</a> of philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_life">Family life</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_children" title="Islam and children">Islam and children</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Islam" title="Women in Islam">Women in Islam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Islam" title="Marriage in Islam">Marriage in Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Minaret_roof_in_constanta.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Minaret_roof_in_constanta.jpg/180px-Minaret_roof_in_constanta.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Minaret_roof_in_constanta.jpg/270px-Minaret_roof_in_constanta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Minaret_roof_in_constanta.jpg/360px-Minaret_roof_in_constanta.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1632" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Minaret_roof_in_constanta.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The dome of the <a href="/wiki/Constan%C8%9Ba_Mosque" class="mw-redirect" title="ConstanÈa Mosque">Carol I Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Constan%C8%9Ba" title="ConstanÈa">ConstanÈa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, topped by the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_crescent" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic crescent">Islamic crescent</a></div></div></div>
<p>In a Muslim family, the birth of a child is attended with some religious ceremonies. Immediately after the birth, the words of <a href="/wiki/Adhan" title="Adhan">Adhan</a> is pronounced in the right ear of the child.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> In the seventh day, the aquiqa ceremony is performed, in which an animal is sacrificed and its meat is distributed among the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-Nigosian-120_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nigosian-120-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> The head of the child is also shaved, and an amount of money equaling the weight of the child's hair is donated to the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-Nigosian-120_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nigosian-120-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Apart from fulfilling the basic needs of food, shelter, and education, the parents or the elderly members of family also undertake the task of teaching moral qualities, religious knowledge, and religious practices to the children.<sup id="cite_ref-Campo-136_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campo-136-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Islam" title="Marriage in Islam">Marriage</a>, which serves as the foundation of a Muslim family, is a civil contract which consists of an offer and acceptance between two qualified parties in the presence of two witnesses. The groom is required to pay a bridal gift (<i><a href="/wiki/Mahr" title="Mahr">mahr</a></i>) to the bride, as stipulated in the contract.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
Most families in the Islamic world are monogamous.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polyandry" title="Polyandry">Polyandry</a>, a practice wherein a woman takes on two or more husbands is prohibited in Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> men are allowed to practice <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">polygyny</a>, that is, they can have more than one wife at the same time, up to a total of four, per <a href="/wiki/Surah" title="Surah">Surah</a> 4 Verse 3. A man does not need approval of his first wife for a second marriage as there is no evidence in the Qur'an or hadith to suggest this. With Muslims coming from diverse backgrounds including 49 Muslim-majority countries, plus a strong presence as large minorities throughout the world there are many variations on Muslim weddings. Generally in a Muslim family, a woman's sphere of operation is the home and a man's corresponding sphere is the outside world. However, in practice, this separation is not as rigid as it appears.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> With regard to inheritance, a son's share is double that of a daughter's.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Certain religious rites are performed during and after the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_death" title="Islamic view of death">death of a Muslim</a>. Those near a dying man encourage him to pronounce the <a href="/wiki/Shahada" title="Shahada">Shahada</a> as Muslims want their last word to be their profession of faith. After the death, the body is appropriately bathed by the members of the same gender and then enshrouded in a threefold white garment called <i>kafan</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Placing the body on a <a href="/wiki/Bier" title="Bier">bier</a>, it is first taken to a mosque where funeral prayer is offered for the dead person, and then to the graveyard for burial.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etiquette_and_diet">Etiquette and diet</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Adab_(Islam)" title="Adab (Islam)">Adab (Islam)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_dietary_laws" title="Islamic dietary laws">Islamic dietary laws</a></div>
<p>Many practices fall in the category of <i>adab</i>, or Islamic etiquette. This includes greeting others with "<i><a href="/wiki/As-Salamu_Alaykum" class="mw-redirect" title="As-Salamu Alaykum">as-salamu 'alaykum</a></i>" ("peace be unto you"), saying <i><a href="/wiki/Basmala" title="Basmala">bismillah</a></i> ("in <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God" title="Names of God">the name of God</a>") before meals, and using only the right hand for eating and drinking. <a href="/wiki/Islamic_hygienical_jurisprudence" title="Islamic hygienical jurisprudence">Islamic hygienic</a> practices mainly fall into the category of personal cleanliness and health. <a href="/wiki/Khitan_(circumcision)" title="Khitan (circumcision)">Circumcision of male offspring</a> is also practiced in Islam. <a href="/wiki/Islamic_funeral" title="Islamic funeral">Islamic burial rituals</a> include saying the <i><a href="/wiki/Salat_al-Janazah" title="Salat al-Janazah">Salat al-Janazah</a></i> ("funeral prayer") over the bathed and enshrouded dead body, and burying it in a <a href="/wiki/Grave_(burial)" class="mw-redirect" title="Grave (burial)">grave</a>. Muslims are restricted in their diet. Prohibited foods include pork products, blood, <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcohol</a>. All meat must come from a <a href="/wiki/Herbivorous" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbivorous">herbivorous</a> animal slaughtered in the name of God by a Muslim, Jew, or Christian, with the exception of game that one has hunted or fished for oneself. Food permissible for Muslims is known as <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a> food.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_responsibilities">Social responsibilities</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_humanity" title="Islam and humanity">Islam and humanity</a></div>
<p>In a Muslim society, various social service activities are performed by the members of the community. As these activities are instructed by <a href="/wiki/Muslim_scriptures" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim scriptures">Islamic canonical texts</a>, a Muslim's religious life is seen incomplete if not attended by service to humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-Stefon92_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stefon92-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> In fact, In Islamic tradition, the idea of social welfare has been presented as one of its principal values.<sup id="cite_ref-Stefon92_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stefon92-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D2%3Averse%3D177">2:177</a> verse of the Quran is often cited to encapsulate the Islamic idea of social welfare.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, duties to parents, neighbors, relatives, sick people, the old, and <a href="/wiki/Minority_group" title="Minority group">minorities</a> have been defined in Islam. Respecting and obeying one's parents, and taking care of them especially in their old age have been made a religious obligation.<sup id="cite_ref-Campo-136_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campo-136-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> A two-fold approach is generally prescribed with regard to duty to <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">relatives</a>: keeping good relations with them, and offering them financial help if necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Severing ties with them has been admonished. Regardless of a neighbor's religious identity, Islam teaches Muslims to treat <a href="/wiki/Neighbourhood" title="Neighbourhood">neighboring people</a> in the best possible manner and not to cause them any difficulty.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Concerning <a href="/wiki/Orphan" title="Orphan">orphaned children</a>, the Quran forbids harsh and oppressive treatment to them while urging kindness and justice towards them. It also rebukes those who do not honor and feed orphaned children (Quran <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D89%3Averse%3D17â18">89:17â18</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Character">Character</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Morality_in_Islam" title="Morality in Islam">Morality in Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salat_Eid_al-Fitr,_Tehran_(113344343).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Salat_Eid_al-Fitr%2C_Tehran_%28113344343%29.jpg/210px-Salat_Eid_al-Fitr%2C_Tehran_%28113344343%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="210" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Salat_Eid_al-Fitr%2C_Tehran_%28113344343%29.jpg/315px-Salat_Eid_al-Fitr%2C_Tehran_%28113344343%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Salat_Eid_al-Fitr%2C_Tehran_%28113344343%29.jpg/420px-Salat_Eid_al-Fitr%2C_Tehran_%28113344343%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="479" data-file-height="392" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salat_Eid_al-Fitr,_Tehran_(113344343).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Hijab" title="Hijab">Hijab</a> represents <a href="/wiki/Morality_in_Islam#Modesty_and_humility" title="Morality in Islam">modesty</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Quran and the <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a> of Muhammad prescribe a comprehensive body of moral guidelines for Muslims to be followed in their personal, social, political, and religious life. Proper moral conduct, good deeds, righteousness, and <a href="/wiki/Moral_character" title="Moral character">good character</a> come within the sphere of the moral guidelines.<sup id="cite_ref-Campo-216_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campo-216-192">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> In Islam, the observance of moral virtues is always associated with religious significance because it elevates the religious status of a <a href="/wiki/Mumin" title="Mumin">believer</a><sup id="cite_ref-Nigosian-116_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nigosian-116-193">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> and is often seen as a supererogatory act of worshipping.<sup id="cite_ref-Leaman-140_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaman-140-194">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> One typical Islamic teaching on <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> is that imposing a penalty on an offender in proportion to their offense is permissible and just; but forgiving the offender is better. To go one step further by offering a favor to the offender is regarded the highest excellence.<sup id="cite_ref-Nigosian-116_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nigosian-116-193">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> The Quran says: 'Repel (evil) with what is best' (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D41%3Averse%3D34">41:34</a>). Thus, a Muslim is expected to act only in good manners as bad manners and deeds earn vices.<sup id="cite_ref-Campo-215_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campo-215-195">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> The fundamental moral qualities in Islam are <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forgiveness" title="Forgiveness">forgiveness</a>, righteousness, kindness, honesty, and piety.<sup id="cite_ref-Campo-216_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campo-216-192">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Other mostly insisted moral virtues include but not limited to charitable activities, fulfillment of promise, <a href="/wiki/Modesty" title="Modesty">modesty</a> (<a href="/wiki/Haya_(Islam)" title="Haya (Islam)">haya</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Humility" title="Humility">humility</a>, decency in speech, tolerance, trustworthiness, <a href="/wiki/Sabr" title="Sabr">patience</a>, truthfulness, <a href="/wiki/Anger_management" title="Anger management">anger management</a>, and sincerity of intention.
</p><p>As a religion, Islam emphasizes the idea of having a good character as Muhammad said: 'The best among you are those who have the best manners and character' (<i><a href="/wiki/Sahih_al-Bukhari" title="Sahih al-Bukhari">Sahih al-Bukhari</a></i>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/19700101010101/http://cmje.usc.edu/religious-texts/hadith/bukhari/073-sbt.php#008.073.056">8:73:56</a>). In Islam, justice is not only a moral virtue but also an obligation to be fulfilled under all circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> The Quran and the hadith describe God as being kind and merciful to His creatures, and tell people to be kind likewise. As a virtue, forgiveness is much celebrated in Islam, and is regarded as an important Muslim practice.<sup id="cite_ref-Leaman-27_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leaman-27-197">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> About modesty, Muhammad is reported as saying: ' Every religion has its characteristic, and the characteristic of Islam is modesty'.<sup id="cite_ref-modesty-1_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modesty-1-198">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Political_aspects_of_Islam" title="Political aspects of Islam">Political aspects of Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_state" title="Islamic state">Islamic state</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_secularism" title="Islam and secularism">Islam and secularism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic democracy">Islamic democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khanate" title="Khanate">Khanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imamate" title="Imamate">Imamate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emirate" title="Emirate">Emirate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mansa_(title)" title="Mansa (title)">Mansa (title)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a></div>
<p>Mainstream Islamic law does not distinguish between "matters of church" and "matters of state"; the <a href="/wiki/Ulema" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulema">scholars</a> function as both jurists and theologians. Currently no government conforms to <a href="/wiki/Islamic_economic_jurisprudence" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic economic jurisprudence">Islamic economic jurisprudence</a>, but steps have been taken to implement some of its tenets.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a> sectarian divide also effects intergovernmental Muslim relations such as between <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20160105-maps_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160105-maps-202">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam" title="History of Islam">History of Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam">Spread of Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin:0 auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:1008px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg" class="image" title="A panoramic view of Al-Masjid al-Nabawi (the Mosque of the Prophet) in Medina, Hejaz region, today&#39;s Saudi Arabia, the second most sacred Mosque in Islam"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg/1000px-Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg" decoding="async" width="1000" height="166" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg/1500px-Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg/2000px-Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2853" data-file-height="475" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg" title="File:Madina Haram at evening.jpg"> </a></div>A panoramic view of <a href="/wiki/Al-Masjid_al-Nabawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Masjid al-Nabawi">Al-Masjid al-Nabawi</a> (the Mosque of the Prophet) in <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> region, today's Saudi Arabia, the second most sacred Mosque in Islam</div></div></div>
