<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:Allah-green.svg" class="image"><img alt="Allah-green.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/15px-Allah-green.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/23px-Allah-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/31px-Allah-green.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="206" data-file-height="215" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam" title="Portal:Islam">Islam&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Muhammad" title="Template:Muhammad"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Muhammad" title="Template talk:Muhammad"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Muhammad&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad in Islam</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_social_changes_under_Islam" title="Early social changes under Islam">Early social changes under Islam</a></div>
<p>Muslim tradition views Muhammad (c. 570 â June 8, 632) as the <a href="/wiki/Seal_of_the_prophets" class="mw-redirect" title="Seal of the prophets">seal of the prophets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> During the last 22 years of his life, beginning at age 40 in 610 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, according to the earliest surviving biographies, Muhammad reported revelations that he believed to be from God, conveyed to him through the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Spirit (Islam)">archangel Gabriel</a>. Muhammad's <a href="/wiki/Sahaba" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahaba">companions</a> memorized and recorded the content of these revelations, known as the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During this time, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Mecca" title="Muhammad in Mecca">Muhammad in Mecca</a> preached to the people, imploring them to abandon polytheism and to worship one God. Although some converted to Islam, the leading Meccan authorities persecuted Muhammad and his followers. This resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Migration_to_Abyssinia" title="Migration to Abyssinia">Migration to Abyssinia</a> of some Muslims (to the <a href="/wiki/Aksumite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Aksumite Empire">Aksumite Empire</a>). Many early converts to Islam were the poor, foreigners and former slaves like <a href="/wiki/Bilal_ibn_Rabah_al-Habashi" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilal ibn Rabah al-Habashi">Bilal ibn Rabah al-Habashi</a> who was black. The Meccan Ã©lite felt that Muhammad was destabilising their social order by preaching about one God and about racial equality, and that in the process he gave ideas to the poor and to their slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After 12 years of the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Muslims_by_the_Meccans" class="mw-redirect" title="Persecution of Muslims by the Meccans">persecution of Muslims by the Meccans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Meccan_boycott_of_the_Hashemites" title="Meccan boycott of the Hashemites">Meccan boycott of the Hashemites</a>, Muhammad's relatives, Muhammad and the Muslims performed the <i><a href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hijra (Islam)">Hijra</a></i> ("emigration") to the city of <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> (formerly known as <i>Yathrib</i>) in 622. There, with the Medinan converts (<i><a href="/wiki/Ansar_(Islam)" title="Ansar (Islam)">Ansar</a></i>) and the Meccan migrants (<i><a href="/wiki/Muhajirun" title="Muhajirun">Muhajirun</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Medina" title="Muhammad in Medina">Muhammad in Medina</a> established his political and <a href="/wiki/Theocracy" title="Theocracy">religious authority</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Medina" title="Constitution of Medina">Constitution of Medina</a> was formulated, instituting a number of rights and responsibilities for the Muslim, Jewish, Christian and pagan communities of Medina, bringing them within the fold of one communityâthe <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Ummah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Constitution established:
</p>
<ul><li>the security of the community</li>
<li>religious freedoms</li>
<li>the role of Medina as a sacred place (barring all violence and weapons)</li>
<li>the security of women</li>
<li>stable tribal relations within Medina</li>
<li>a tax system for supporting the community in time of conflict</li>
<li>parameters for exogenous political alliances</li>
<li>a system for granting protection of individuals</li>
<li>a judicial system for resolving disputes where non-Muslims could also use their own laws and have their own judges.<sup id="cite_ref-B._Serjeant_1978_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B._Serjeant_1978-211">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watt_1964_p.4_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watt_1964_p.4-212">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Constitution_of_Medina_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution_of_Medina-213">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>All the tribes signed the agreement to defend Medina from all external threats and to live in harmony amongst themselves. Within a few years, two battles took place against the Meccan forces: first, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Badr" title="Battle of Badr">Battle of Badr</a> in 624âa Muslim victory, and then a year later, when the Meccans returned to Medina, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Uhud" title="Battle of Uhud">Battle of Uhud</a>, which ended inconclusively.
</p><p>The Arab tribes in the rest of Arabia then formed a confederation and during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Trench" title="Battle of the Trench">Battle of the Trench</a> (MarchâApril 627) besieged Medina, intent on finishing off Islam. In 628, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Hudaybiyyah" title="Treaty of Hudaybiyyah">Treaty of Hudaybiyyah</a> was signed between Mecca and the Muslims and was broken by Mecca two years later. After the signing of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah many more people converted to Islam. At the same time, Meccan trade routes were cut off as Muhammad brought surrounding desert tribes under his control.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> By 629 Muhammad was victorious in the nearly bloodless <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Mecca" title="Conquest of Mecca">conquest of Mecca</a>, and by the time of his death in 632 (at the age of 62) he had united the <a href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Arabia" title="Tribes of Arabia">tribes of Arabia</a> into a single religious <a href="/wiki/Polity" title="Polity">polity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-215">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest three generations of Muslims are known as the <a href="/wiki/Salaf" title="Salaf">Salaf</a>, with the companions of Muhammad being known as the <a href="/wiki/Sahaba" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahaba">Sahaba</a>. Many of them, such as the largest narrator of hadith <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hureyrah" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu Hureyrah">Abu Hureyrah</a>, recorded and compiled what would constitute the sunnah.
</p>
<h3><span id="Caliphate_and_civil_strife_.28632.E2.80.93750.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Caliphate_and_civil_strife_(632â750)">Caliphate and civil strife (632â750)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">Succession to Muhammad</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_event_of_Ghadir_Khumm" class="mw-redirect" title="The event of Ghadir Khumm">The event of Ghadir Khumm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saqifa" class="mw-redirect" title="Saqifa">Saqifa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_Fitna" title="First Fitna">First Fitna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Second_Fitna" title="Second Fitna">Second Fitna</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mohammad_adil-Rashidun_empire-slide.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Mohammad_adil-Rashidun_empire-slide.gif/220px-Mohammad_adil-Rashidun_empire-slide.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Mohammad_adil-Rashidun_empire-slide.gif/330px-Mohammad_adil-Rashidun_empire-slide.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Mohammad_adil-Rashidun_empire-slide.gif/440px-Mohammad_adil-Rashidun_empire-slide.gif 2x" data-file-width="1196" data-file-height="580" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mohammad_adil-Rashidun_empire-slide.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a> expansion</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg/220px-Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg/330px-Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg/440px-Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dome_of_the_Rock1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dome_of_the_Rock" title="Dome of the Rock">Dome of the Rock</a> built by <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Malik_ibn_Marwan" title="Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan">Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan</a>; completed at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Fitna" title="Second Fitna">Second Fitna</a></div></div></div>
<p>With Muhammad's death in 632, disagreement broke out over who would succeed him as leader of the Muslim community. <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, a companion and close friend of Muhammad, was made the first <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">caliph</a>. Under Abu Bakr, Muslims put down a rebellion by Arab tribes in an episode known as the <a href="/wiki/Ridda_wars" title="Ridda wars">Ridda wars</a>, or "Wars of Apostasy".<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> The Quran was compiled into a single volume at this time.
</p><p>Abu Bakr's death in 634 resulted in the succession of <a href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_al-Khattab" class="mw-redirect" title="Umar ibn al-Khattab">Umar ibn al-Khattab</a> as the caliph, followed by <a href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_al-Affan" class="mw-redirect" title="Uthman ibn al-Affan">Uthman ibn al-Affan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">Hasan ibn Ali</a>. The first four caliphs are known in Sunni Islam as <i>al-khulafÄ' ar-rÄshidÅ«n</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Rightly_Guided_Caliphs" class="mw-redirect" title="Rightly Guided Caliphs">Rightly Guided Caliphs</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Under them, the territory under Muslim rule expanded deeply into the parts of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid Empire">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> territories.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When Umar was assassinated by Persians in 644, <a href="/wiki/The_election_of_Uthman" class="mw-redirect" title="The election of Uthman">the election of Uthman</a> as successor was met with increasing opposition. The standard copies of the Quran were also distributed throughout the Islamic State. In 656, Uthman was also killed, and <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali</a> assumed the position of caliph. This led to the <a href="/wiki/First_Fitna" title="First Fitna">first civil war</a> (the "First Fitna") over who should be caliph. Ali was assassinated by <a href="/wiki/Kharijites" class="mw-redirect" title="Kharijites">Kharijites</a> in 661. To avoid further fighting, the new caliph <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">Hasan ibn Ali</a> signed a <a href="/wiki/Hasan%E2%80%93Muawiya_treaty" title="HasanâMuawiya treaty">peace treaty</a>, abdicating to <a href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Mu'awiyah</a>, beginning the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_dynasty" title="Umayyad dynasty">Umayyad dynasty</a>, in return that he not name his own successor.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> These disputes over religious and political leadership would give rise to schism in the Muslim community. The majority accepted the legitimacy of the first four leaders and became known as Sunnis. A minority disagreed, and believed that only Ali and some of his descendants should rule; they became known as the Shia.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Mu'awiyah appointed his son, <a href="/wiki/Yazid_I" title="Yazid I">Yazid I</a>, as successor and after Mu'awiyah's death in 680, the "<a href="/wiki/Second_Fitna" title="Second Fitna">Second Fitna</a>" broke out, where <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">Husayn ibn Ali</a> was killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Karbala" title="Battle of Karbala">Battle of Karbala</a>, a significant event in Shia Islam. <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a> thus differ in some respects.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20160103_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160103-221">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Umayyad dynasty conquered the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Narbonensis" title="Gallia Narbonensis">Narbonnese Gaul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Local populations of Jews and indigenous Christians, persecuted as religious minorities and taxed heavily to finance the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sassanid_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="ByzantineâSassanid Wars">ByzantineâSassanid Wars</a>, often aided Muslims to take over their lands from the Byzantines and Persians, resulting in exceptionally speedy conquests.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The generation after the death of Muhammad but contemporaries of his companions are known as the <a href="/wiki/Tabi%27un" title="Tabi&#39;un">Tabi'un</a>, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Tabi%E2%80%98_al-Tabi%E2%80%98in" class="mw-redirect" title="Tabiâ al-Tabiâin">Tabiâ al-Tabiâin</a>. The Caliph <a href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_Abd_al-Aziz" class="mw-redirect" title="Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz">Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz</a> set up the influential committee, "<a href="/wiki/The_Seven_Fuqaha_of_Medina" title="The Seven Fuqaha of Medina">The Seven Fuqaha of Medina</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> headed by <a href="/wiki/Qasim_ibn_Muhammad_ibn_Abu_Bakr" title="Qasim ibn Muhammad ibn Abu Bakr">Qasim ibn Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malik_ibn_Anas" title="Malik ibn Anas">Malik ibn Anas</a> wrote one of the earliest books on Islamic jurisprudence, the <a href="/wiki/Muwatta_Imam_Malik" title="Muwatta Imam Malik">Muwatta</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-bewley.virtualave.net_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bewley.virtualave.net-228">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> as a consensus of the opinion of those jurists.<sup id="cite_ref-Coulson_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coulson-229">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The descendants of Muhammad's uncle <a href="/wiki/Abbas_ibn_Abd_al-Muttalib" title="Abbas ibn Abd al-Muttalib">Abbas ibn Abd al-Muttalib</a> rallied discontented non-Arab converts (<i><a href="/wiki/Mawali" class="mw-redirect" title="Mawali">mawali</a></i>), poor Arabs, and some Shi'a against the Umayyads and overthrew them, inaugurating the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasid">Abbasid dynasty</a> in 750.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first Muslim states independent of a unified Islamic state emerged from the Berber Revolt (739/740-743).
</p>
<h3><span id="Classical_era_.28750.E2.80.931258.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_era_(750â1258)">Classical era (750â1258)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadith_studies" title="Hadith studies">Hadith studies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_world_contributions_to_Medieval_Europe" title="Islamic world contributions to Medieval Europe">Islamic world contributions to Medieval Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turco-Persian_tradition" title="Turco-Persian tradition">Turco-Persian tradition</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/220px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/330px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/440px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The eye, according to <a href="/wiki/Hunain_ibn_Ishaq" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunain ibn Ishaq">Hunain ibn Ishaq</a> from a manuscript dated circa 1200</div></div></div>
<p>Al-Shafi'i codified a method to determine the reliability of hadith.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> During the early Abbasid era, the major <a href="/wiki/Six_major_Hadith_collections" class="mw-redirect" title="Six major Hadith collections">Sunni hadith collections</a> were compiled by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Bukhari" title="Muhammad al-Bukhari">Bukhari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim_ibn_al-Hajjaj" title="Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj">Muslim</a> while major <a href="/wiki/The_Four_Books" title="The Four Books">Shia hadith collections</a> by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Ya%27qub_al-Kulayni" title="Muhammad ibn Ya&#39;qub al-Kulayni">Al-Kulayni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Babawayh" title="Ibn Babawayh">Ibn Babawayh</a> were also compiled. The <a href="/wiki/Ja%27fari_jurisprudence" title="Ja&#39;fari jurisprudence">Ja'fari jurisprudence</a> was formed from the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Ja%27far_al-Sadiq" title="Ja&#39;far al-Sadiq">Ja'far al-Sadiq</a> while the four Sunni <a href="/wiki/Madh%27hab" class="mw-redirect" title="Madh&#39;hab">Madh'habs</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Shafi&#39;i">Shafi'i</a>, were established around the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_%E1%B8%A4an%C4%ABfa" class="mw-redirect" title="AbÅ« á¸¤anÄ«fa">AbÅ« á¸¤anÄ«fa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_bin_Hanbal" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmad bin Hanbal">Ahmad bin Hanbal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malik_ibn_Anas" title="Malik ibn Anas">Malik ibn Anas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Shafi%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Shafi&#39;i">al-Shafi'i</a> respectively. In the 9th century, al-Shafi'i provided a theoretical basis for Islamic law and introduced its first methods by a synthesis between proto-rationalism of <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraqian</a> jurisprudence and the pragmatic approach of the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> traditions, in his book <i>ar-RisÄlah</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> However, Islamic law was not codified until 1869.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> In the 9th century <a href="/wiki/Al-Tabari" title="Al-Tabari">Al-Tabari</a> completed the first commentary of the Quran, that became one of the most cited commentaries in <a href="/wiki/Sunnism" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunnism">Sunni Islam</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tafsir_al-Tabari" title="Tafsir al-Tabari">Tafsir al-Tabari</a>. During its expansion through the <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanid Empire</a>, Islam was shaped by the ethno-cultural and religious pluralism by the <a href="/wiki/Sogdians" class="mw-redirect" title="Sogdians">Sogdians</a>, paving the way for a <i>Persianized</i> rather than <i>Arabized</i> understanding of Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some Muslims began to question the piety of indulgence in a worldly life and emphasised poverty, humility and avoidance of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_sin" title="Islamic views on sin">sin</a> based on renunciation of bodily desires. Ascetics such as <a href="/wiki/Hasan_al-Basri" title="Hasan al-Basri">Hasan al-Basri</a> would inspire a movement that would evolve into Tasawwuf (Sufism).<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of the 9th century, the <a href="/wiki/Ismailism" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismailism">Ismaili</a> spread in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, whereupon the city <a href="/wiki/Multan" title="Multan">Multan</a> became target by <a href="/wiki/Activism" title="Activism">activistic</a> Sunni politics.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> In 930, the Ismaili group known as the <a href="/wiki/Qarmatians" title="Qarmatians">Qarmatians</a> unsuccessfully rebelled against the Abbassids, sacked Mecca and stole the Black Stone, which was eventually retrieved.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Caliphs such as <a href="/wiki/Mamun_al_Rashid" class="mw-redirect" title="Mamun al Rashid">Mamun al Rashid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27tasim" title="Al-Mu&#39;tasim">Al-Mu'tasim</a> made the <a href="/wiki/Mutazilite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutazilite">mutazilite</a> philosophy an official creed and imposed it upon Muslims to follow. Mu'tazila was a Greek influenced school<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="is it only Greek? or did it incorperate other ideas as well? And I gues Greek is too broad, since it was rather Neo-Platonism (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> of Sunni scholastic theology called <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">kalam</a>, which refers to <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">dialectic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Many orthodox Muslims<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2019)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> rejected <a href="/wiki/Mutazilite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutazilite">mutazilite</a> doctrines and condemned their idea of the creation of the Quran. In inquisitions, ibn Hanbal refused to conform<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Was he imprisioned for theological or political reasons? (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and was tortured and sent to an unlit <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> prison cell for nearly thirty months.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Other branches of kalam were the <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" class="mw-redirect" title="Ash&#39;ari">Ash'ari</a> school founded by <a href="/wiki/Al-Ash%27ari" title="Al-Ash&#39;ari">Al-Ash'ari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidi</a> founded by <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansur_al-Maturidi" title="Abu Mansur al-Maturidi">Abu Mansur al-Maturidi</a>.
</p><p>With the expansion of the Abbaside Caliphate into the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>, Islam adapted many <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_philosophy" title="Hellenistic philosophy">Hellenistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">Persian</a> concepts, imported by thinkers of <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> origin.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> sought to incorporate <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Greek principles</a> into Islamic theology, while others like <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a> argued against such <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a> and ultimately prevailed.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Avicenna pioneered the science of <a href="/wiki/Medical_research" title="Medical research">experimental medicine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Jacquart,_Danielle_2008_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacquart,_Danielle_2008-245">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> and was the first <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> to conduct <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trials" class="mw-redirect" title="Clinical trials">clinical trials</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> His two most notable works, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Healing" title="The Book of Healing">The Book of Healing</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">The Canon of Medicine</a></i>, were used as standard medicinal texts in the Islamic world and later in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. Amongst his contributions are the discovery of the contagious nature of infectious diseases,<sup id="cite_ref-Jacquart,_Danielle_2008_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacquart,_Danielle_2008-245">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> and the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Clinical_pharmacology" title="Clinical pharmacology">clinical pharmacology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Islamic_mathematics" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic mathematics">mathematics</a>, the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Musa_al-Khwarizmi" title="Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi">Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi</a> gave his name to the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithm</a>, while the term <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a> is derived from <i>al-jabr</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Persian_literature" title="Persian literature">Persian poet</a> <a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a> wrote his <a href="/wiki/Epic_poem" class="mw-redirect" title="Epic poem">epic poem</a> <a href="/wiki/Shahnameh" title="Shahnameh">Shahnameh</a>. <a href="/wiki/Rumi" title="Rumi">Rumi</a> wrote some of the finest Persian poetry and is still one of the best selling poets in America.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Legal institutions introduced include the <a href="/wiki/Trust_law" title="Trust law">trust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charitable_trust" title="Charitable trust">charitable trust</a> (<a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">Waqf</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This era is sometimes called the "<a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Public_hospital" title="Public hospital">Public hospitals</a> established during this time (called <a href="/wiki/Bimaristan" title="Bimaristan">Bimaristan</a> hospitals), are considered "the first hospitals" in the modern sense of the word,<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> and issued the first medical diplomas to license <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">doctors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Alatas_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alatas-256">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a> recognizes the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Al_Karaouine" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Al Karaouine">University of Al Karaouine</a>, founded in 859, as the world's oldest degree-granting university.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> The doctorate is argued to date back to the <a href="/wiki/Ijazah" title="Ijazah">licenses to teach</a> in Islamic <a href="/wiki/Law_school" title="Law school">law schools</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Makdisi_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makdisi-259">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> Standards of experimental and <a href="/wiki/Quantification_(science)" title="Quantification (science)">quantification</a> techniques, as well as the tradition of citation,<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed-260">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> were introduced. An important pioneer in this, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Ibn al-Haytham</a> is regarded as the father of the modern <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> and often referred to as the "world's first true scientist".<sup id="cite_ref-Haq_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haq-261">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> The government paid scientists the equivalent salary of professional athletes today.<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed_260-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed-260">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> It is argued that the data used by <a href="/wiki/Copernicus" class="mw-redirect" title="Copernicus">Copernicus</a> for his <a href="/wiki/Heliocentric" class="mw-redirect" title="Heliocentric">heliocentric</a> conclusions was gathered and that <a href="/wiki/Al-Jahiz" title="Al-Jahiz">Al-Jahiz</a> proposed a theory of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the Abbasid Caliphate suffered a decline since the reign of <a href="/wiki/Al-Wathiq" title="Al-Wathiq">Al-Wathiq</a> (842â847) and <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27tadid" title="Al-Mu&#39;tadid">Al-Mu'tadid</a> (892â902),<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> put an end to the Abbassid dynasty in 1258.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> During its decline, the Abbasid Caliphate disintegrated into minor states and dynasties, such as the <a href="/wiki/Tulunid" class="mw-redirect" title="Tulunid">Tulunid</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghaznavid">Ghaznavid dynasty</a>. The Ghaznavid dynasty was an Islamic dynasty established by Turkic slave-soldiers from another Islamic empire, the <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanid Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Two <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkish</a> tribes, the <a href="/wiki/Kara-Khanid_Khanate" title="Kara-Khanid Khanate">Karahanids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuks</a>, converted to Islam during the 10th century, who are later subdued by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a>, who share the same origin and language. It is important to note, that the following Islamic reign by the Ottomans was strongly influenced by a <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiosis</a> between <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman rulers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a> since the beginning. According to Ottoman historiography, the legitimation of a ruler is attributed to <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Edebali" title="Sheikh Edebali">Sheikh Edebali</a>. Accordingly, he interpretated a dream of <a href="/wiki/Osman_I" title="Osman I">Osman Gazi</a> as God's legitimation of his reign.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mevlevi_Order" title="Mevlevi Order">Mevlevi Order</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bektashi_Order" title="Bektashi Order">Bektashi Order</a> had close relation to the sultans.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>  The Seljuks played an important role for the revival of Sunnism, then Shia increased its influences. The Seljuk militar leader <a href="/wiki/Alp_Arslan" title="Alp Arslan">Alp Arslan</a> financially supported sciences and literature and established the <a href="/wiki/Nezamiyeh" title="Nezamiyeh">Nezamiyeh</a> university in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During this time, the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> took over northern parts of the Indian subcontinent. Religious missions converted <a href="/wiki/Volga_Bulgaria" title="Volga Bulgaria">Volga Bulgaria</a> to Islam. Many Muslims also went to <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_China" title="Islam in China">China</a> to trade, virtually dominating the import and export industry of the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-china_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-china-273">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Pre-Modern_era_.281258.E2.80.9318th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Modern_era_(1258â18th_century)">Pre-Modern era (1258â18th century)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/220px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/330px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/440px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1185" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Abd%C3%BClmecid_II" class="mw-redirect" title="AbdÃ¼lmecid II">AbdÃ¼lmecid II</a> was the last Caliph of Islam from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty" title="Ottoman dynasty">Ottoman dynasty</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Islam spread with Muslim trade networks and Sufi orders activity that extended into <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, Central Asia and the <a href="/wiki/Malay_archipelago" class="mw-redirect" title="Malay archipelago">Malay archipelago</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EoI-Islam_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Islam-274">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> Under the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, Islam spread to Southeast Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> Throughout this expanse, Islam blended with local cultures everywhere, as illustrated when the prophet Mohammed showed up in Hindu epics and folklore.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> Conversion to Islam, however, was not a sudden abandonment of old religious practices; rather, it was typically a matter of "assimilating Islamic rituals, cosmologies, and literatures into... local religious systems."<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> The Muslims in China who were descended from earlier immigration began to assimilate by adopting Chinese names and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">culture</a> while <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> became an important center of Islamic study.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Turks incorporated elements of <a href="/wiki/Tengrism" title="Tengrism">Turkish Shamanism</a> into their new religion and became part of a new Islamic interpretation,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceWorldWide_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceWorldWide-281">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> although  Shamanistic influences already occurred during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Talas" title="Battle of Talas">Battle of Talas</a> (752). Strikingly, Shamans were never mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Heresiology" title="Heresiology">Muslim Heresiographers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> One major change was the status of woman. Unlike Arabic traditions, the Turkic traditions hold woman in higher regard in society.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceWorldWide_281-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceWorldWide-281">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> Turks preserved this status of woman even after conversion to Islam. Further, the Turks must have found striking similarities between the Sufi rituals and Shaman practises.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceWorldWide_281-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceWorldWide-281">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> However, the influence of Turkish belief was not limited to Sufism, but also to Muslims who subscribed an orthodox version of Islam in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central-Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Central-Asia">Central-Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balkan" class="mw-redirect" title="Balkan">Balkans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceWorldWide_281-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceWorldWide-281">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> As a result, many (formerly) Shaman traditions were considered as genuine Islamic by average Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceWorldWide_281-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceWorldWide-281">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> Many shamanistic beliefs, such as the belief in sacred nature, trees and animals, and foreign <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0ye" title="Ä°ye">nature spirits</a>, even remained today.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority and oldest group among Shia at that time, the <a href="/wiki/Zaydis" class="mw-redirect" title="Zaydis">Zaydis</a>, named after the great grandson of Ali, the scholar <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Ali" title="Zayd ibn Ali">Zayd ibn Ali</a>, used the Hanafi jurisprudence, as did most Sunnis.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.co.uk-mae_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.co.uk-mae-284">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arab-Israeli_Conflict_Page_917_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arab-Israeli_Conflict_Page_917-285">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Iraq_Effect_Page_91_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Iraq_Effect_Page_91-286">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> The Shia <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> rose to power in 1501 and later conquered all of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> The ensuing mandatory <a href="/wiki/Safavid_conversion_of_Iran_to_Shia_Islam" title="Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam">conversion of Iran to Twelver Shia Islam</a> for the largely Sunni population also ensured the final dominance of the Twelver sect within Shiism over the <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaidi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ismaili" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismaili">Ismaili</a> sects.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader Shah</a>, who overthrew the Safavids, attempted to improve relations with Sunnis by propagating the integration of Shiism by calling it the Jaafari Madh'hab.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyya" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Taymiyya">Ibn Taymiyya</a> (1263â1328) worried about the integrity of Islam and tried to establish a theological doctrine to purify Islam from its alleged alterings.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-290">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> Unlike his contemporary scholarship, who relied on traditions and historical narratives from early Islam, Ibn Taymiyya's methodology was a mixture of selective use of hadith and a literal understanding of the Quran.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_290-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-290">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-291">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> He rejected most philosophical approaches of Islam and proposed a clear, simple and dogmatic theology instead.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_290-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-290">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> Another major characteristic of his theological approach emphazises the significance of a <a href="/wiki/Theocratic_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Theocratic state">Theocratic state</a>: While the prevailing opinion held that religious wisdom was necessary for a state, Ibn Taymiyya regarded <a href="/wiki/Power_(social_and_political)" title="Power (social and political)">Political power</a> as necessary for religious excellence.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_290-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-290">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> He further rejected many hadiths circulating among Muslims during his time and relied only on <a href="/wiki/Sahih_Bukhari" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahih Bukhari">Sahih Bukhari</a> <i>and</i> <a href="/wiki/Sahih_Muslim" title="Sahih Muslim">Sahih Muslim</a> repeatedly to foil <a href="/wiki/Ash%CA%BFari" title="AshÊ¿ari">Asharite</a> doctrine.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_291-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-291">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> Feeling threatened by the Crusaders as well as by the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_the_Levant" title="Mongol invasions of the Levant">Mongols</a>, Ibn Taymiyya stated it would be obligated to Muslims to join a physical <a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">jihad</a> against <i>unbelievers</i>. This not only including the invaders, but also the <i>heretics</i> among the Muslims, including <a href="/wiki/Shia" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia">Shias</a>, Asharites and "philosophers", who were blamed by Ibn Taimiya for the deterioration of Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceD_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceD-293">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, his writings only played a marginal role during his lifetime. He was repeatedly accused of blasphemy by <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphizing</a> God and his disciple <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Kathir" title="Ibn Kathir">Ibn Kathir</a> distanced himself from his mentor and negated the anthropomorphizations,<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> but simultaneously adhered to anti-rationalistic and hadith oriented methodology of his former mentor.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> This probably influenced his exegesis on his <a href="/wiki/Tafsir_Ibn_Kathir" class="mw-redirect" title="Tafsir Ibn Kathir">Tafsir</a>, which discounted much of the exegetical tradition since then.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> However, the writings of Ibn Taimiyya became important sources for <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabism</a> and 21th century <a href="/wiki/Salafism" class="mw-redirect" title="Salafism">Salafi theology</a><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceD_293-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceD-293">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_290-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-290">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_291-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-291">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> just like <i>Tafsir Ibn Kathir</i> became highly rewarded in modern Salafism.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Modern_era_.2818th_.E2.80.93_20th_centuries.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era_(18th_â_20th_centuries)">Modern era (18th â 20th centuries)</span></h3>
<p>The Muslim world was generally in political decline starting the 1800s, especially relative to the non-Muslim European powers. This decline was evident culturally; while <a href="/wiki/Taqi_al-Din_Muhammad_ibn_Ma%27ruf" class="mw-redirect" title="Taqi al-Din Muhammad ibn Ma&#39;ruf">Taqi al-Din</a> founded an observatory in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> and the Jai Singh Observatory was built in the 18th century, there was not a single Muslim-majority country with a major observatory by the twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a>, launched against Muslim <a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">principalities</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberia</a>, succeeded in 1492. By the 19th century the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Empire</a> had formally ended the Mughal dynasty in India.<sup id="cite_ref-Lapidus_2002,_pp.358,378â380,624_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lapidus_2002,_pp.358,378â380,624-300">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> In the 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Deobandi" title="Deobandi">Deobandi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barelwi" class="mw-redirect" title="Barelwi">Barelwi</a> movements were initiated.
</p><p>During the 18th century <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Abd_al-Wahhab" title="Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab">Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab</a> founded a military movement opposing the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Sultanate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman Sultanate">Ottoman Sultanate</a> as an illegitimate rule, advising his fellows to return to the principles of Islam based on the theology of <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceE_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceE-302">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> He was deeply influenced by the works of Ibn Taymiyya and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Qayyim" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn al-Qayyim">Ibn al-Qayyim</a> and condemned many traditional Islamic practises, such as visiting the <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">grave of Muhammad</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wali" title="Wali">Saints</a>, as sin.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceE_302-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceE-302">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> During the 18th century, he formed an alliance with the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Saud" title="House of Saud">Saud family</a>, who founded the <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabi sect</a>. This revival movement allegedly seeks to uphold monotheism and purify Islam of what they see as later <a href="/wiki/Bidah" class="mw-redirect" title="Bidah">innovations</a>. Their ideology led to the desecration of shrines around the world, including that of <a href="/wiki/Destruction_of_early_Islamic_heritage_sites_in_Saudi_Arabia" title="Destruction of early Islamic heritage sites in Saudi Arabia">Muhammad and his companions in Mecca and Medina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalists</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Rashid_Rida" title="Rashid Rida">Rashid Rida</a>, regarded the Khalifat as an Arabic right taken away by the Turks. Therefore, they rebelled against the Ottoman Sultanate, until the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of the Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire disintegrated</a> after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a> was abolished in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> Concurrently <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Saud" title="Ibn Saud">Ibn Saud</a> conquered <a href="/wiki/Mekka" class="mw-redirect" title="Mekka">Mekka</a>, the "heartland of Islam", to impose Wahhabism as part of Islamic culture.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of the 19th century, Muslim luminaries such as <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Abduh" title="Muhammad Abduh">Muhammad Abduh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rashid_Rida" title="Rashid Rida">Rashid Rida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jamal_al-Din_al-Afghani" class="mw-redirect" title="Jamal al-Din al-Afghani">Jamal al-Din al-Afghani</a> sought to reconcile Islam with social and intellectual ideas of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a> by purging Islam from alleged alterations and adhering to the basic tenets held during the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Rashidun</a> era.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceF_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceF-307">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> Due to their adherence to the <i><a href="/wiki/Salaf" title="Salaf">Salafs</a></i> they called themselves <i>Salafiyya</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceG_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceG-308">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceF_307-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceF-307">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> However, they differ from the Salafi movement flourishing in the second half of the 20th century, which is rooted in the Wahhabi movement. Instead, they are also often called <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Modernism" title="Islamic Modernism">Islamic modernists</a>. They rejected the <a href="/wiki/Sunni_schools_of_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni schools of law">Sunni schools of law</a> and allowed <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">Ijtihad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceG_308-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceG-308">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ahle Sunnat movement, or as it is more popularly known, the <a href="/wiki/Barelwi" class="mw-redirect" title="Barelwi">Barelwi</a> movement emphasizes the primacy of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a> over adherence to Sufi practices and personal devotion to the prophet Muhammad.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> It grew from the writings of muhaddith and jurist Imam <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Raza_Khan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmed Raza Khan">Ahmed Raza Khan</a> Qadri, Allama Fazle Haq Khairabadi,
<a href="/wiki/Shah_Ahmad_Noorani" title="Shah Ahmad Noorani">Shah Ahmad Noorani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Abdul_Ghafoor_Hazarvi" title="Mohammad Abdul Ghafoor Hazarvi">Mohammad Abdul Ghafoor Hazarvi</a> in the backdrop of an intellectual and moral decline of Muslims in British India.<sup id="cite_ref-Reference2011_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reference2011-310">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> The movement was a mass movement, defending popular <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a> and reforming its practices, grew in response to the radical Deobandi movement in South Asia and the <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabi movement</a> elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-Canfield2002_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canfield2002-311">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup>
The movement opposed <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmadiyya Movement">Ahmadiyya Movement</a> and is famous for the celebration of <a href="/wiki/Mawlid" title="Mawlid">Mawlid</a>. Today the movement is spread across the globe with followers in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, United States, and UK among other countries. The movement now has over 200 million followers.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Postmodern_times_.2820th_century.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Postmodern_times_(20th_centuryâpresent)">Postmodern times (20th centuryâpresent)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_revival" title="Islamic revival">Islamic revival</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_OIC.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_OIC.svg/220px-Flag_of_OIC.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_OIC.svg/330px-Flag_of_OIC.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_OIC.svg/440px-Flag_of_OIC.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_OIC.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The flag of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Contact with industrialized nations brought Muslim populations to new areas through economic migration. Many Muslims migrated as indentured servants, from mostly India and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, forming the largest Muslim populations by percentage in the Americas.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> The resulting urbanization and increase in trade in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> brought Muslims to settle in new areas and spread their faith, likely doubling its Muslim population between 1869 and 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> Muslim immigrants began arriving, many as <a href="/wiki/Guest_workers" class="mw-redirect" title="Guest workers">guest workers</a> and largely from former colonies, in several Western European nations since the 1960s.
</p><p>There are more and more new Muslim intellectuals who increasingly separate perennial Islamic beliefs from archaic cultural traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal Islam">Liberal Islam</a> is a movement that attempts to reconcile religious tradition with modern norms of secular governance and human rights. Its supporters say that there are multiple ways to read Islam's sacred texts, and they stress the need to leave room for "independent thought on religious matters".<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup>
Women's issues receive significant weight in the modern discourse on Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-Rippin_2001,_p.288_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rippin_2001,_p.288-317">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Secular powers such as the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Red_Guards_(China)" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Guards (China)">Red Guards</a> closed many mosques and destroyed Qurans,<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Socialist_Republic_of_Albania" title="People&#39;s Socialist Republic of Albania">Communist Albania</a> became the first country to ban the practice of every religion.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> About half a million Muslims were killed in <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Rouge" title="Khmer Rouge">Cambodia</a> by communists who, it is argued, viewed them as their primary enemy and wished to exterminate them since they stood out and worshipped their own god.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the military carried out coups to oust Islamist governments, and headscarves were banned in official buildings, as also happened in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jamal-al-Din_al-Afghani" class="mw-redirect" title="Jamal-al-Din al-Afghani">Jamal-al-Din al-Afghani</a>, along with his acolyte <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Abduh" title="Muhammad Abduh">Muhammad Abduh</a>, have been credited as forerunners of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_revival" title="Islamic revival">Islamic revival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abul_A%27la_Maududi" title="Abul A&#39;la Maududi">Abul A'la Maududi</a> helped influence modern <a href="/wiki/Political_Islam" title="Political Islam">political Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islamist" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamist">Islamist</a> groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a> advocate Islam as a comprehensive political solution, often in spite of being banned.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">revolution</a> replaced a <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a> regime with an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_state" title="Islamic state">Islamic state</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the Islamist <a href="/wiki/Justice_and_Development_Party_(Turkey)" title="Justice and Development Party (Turkey)">AK Party</a> has democratically been in power for about a decade, while Islamist parties did well in elections following the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a> (OIC), consisting of <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">Muslim-majority countries</a>, was established in 1969 after the burning of the <a href="/wiki/Al-Aqsa_Mosque" title="Al-Aqsa Mosque">Al-Aqsa Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Religiosity appears to be deepening worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-economist_329-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-329">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> In many places, the prevalence of the <a href="/wiki/Hijab" title="Hijab">hijab</a> is growing increasingly common<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> and the percentage of Muslims favoring Sharia has increased.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> With religious guidance increasingly available electronically, Muslims are able to access views that are strict enough for them rather than rely on state clerics who are often seen as stooges.<sup id="cite_ref-economist_329-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-329">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is estimated that, by 2050, the number of Muslims will nearly equal the number of Christians around the world, "driven primarily by differences in fertility rates and the size of youth populations among the world's major religions, as well as by people switching faiths."<sup id="cite_ref-pew2015_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew2015-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps as a sign of these changes, most experts agree that Islam is growing faster than any other faith in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East</a> and West Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Denominations">Denominations</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches" title="Islamic schools and branches">Islamic schools and branches</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations" title="ShiaâSunni relations">ShiaâSunni relations</a></div>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Islam_branches_and_schools.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Islam_branches_and_schools.svg/750px-Islam_branches_and_schools.svg.png" decoding="async" width="750" height="488" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Islam_branches_and_schools.svg/1125px-Islam_branches_and_schools.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Islam_branches_and_schools.svg/1500px-Islam_branches_and_schools.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1032" data-file-height="672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Islam_branches_and_schools.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An overview of the major schools and branches of Islam.</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunni">Sunni</span></h3>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks collapsible collapsed" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;font-size:65%;"><span style="padding-left:2.25em;">&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/File:Basmala.svg" class="image" title="bi-smi llÄhi r-raá¸¥mÄni r-raá¸¥Ä«m"><img alt="bi-smi llÄhi r-raá¸¥mÄni r-raá¸¥Ä«m" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basmala.svg/195px-Basmala.svg.png" decoding="async" width="195" height="38" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basmala.svg/293px-Basmala.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basmala.svg/390px-Basmala.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="608" data-file-height="119" /></a>
