</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
<a href="/wiki/File:Allah-green.svg" class="image"><img alt="Allah-green.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/15px-Allah-green.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/23px-Allah-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/31px-Allah-green.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="206" data-file-height="215" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam" title="Portal:Islam">Islam&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Sunni_Islam" title="Template:Sunni Islam"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Sunni_Islam" title="Template talk:Sunni Islam"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Sunni_Islam&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png/220px-Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png/330px-Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png/440px-Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png 2x" data-file-width="908" data-file-height="835" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sahih_Al-Bukhari_in_English.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sahih_Al-Bukhari" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahih Al-Bukhari">Sahih Al-Bukhari</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Al-Kutub_Al-Sittah" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Kutub Al-Sittah">six Sunni hadith books</a>.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islam</a></div>
<p>The largest denomination in Islam is Sunni Islam, which makes up 75â90% of all Muslims<sup id="cite_ref-Sunni-eb_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sunni-eb-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and is arguably the world's largest religious denomination.<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> Sunni Muslims also go by the name <i>Ahl as-Sunnah</i> which means "people of the tradition [of Muhammad]".<sup id="cite_ref-USNewsLippman_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USNewsLippman-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-336">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sunnis believe that the first four <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">caliphs</a> were the rightful successors to Muhammad; since God did not specify any particular leaders to succeed him and those leaders were elected. Further authorities regarding Sunnis believe that anyone who is righteous and just could be a caliph as long they act according to the teachings of Islam, the example of Muhammad. Alternatively, Sunnis commonly accept the companions of Muhammad as reliable for interpretating Islamic affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sunnis follow the Quran and the Hadith, which are recorded in sunni traditions known as <a href="/wiki/Al-Kutub_Al-Sittah" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Kutub Al-Sittah">Al-Kutub Al-Sittah</a> (six major books). For legal matters derived from the Quran or the Hadith, many follow four sunni <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">madh'habs</a> (schools of thought): <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Shafi&#39;i">Shafi'i</a>. All four accept the validity of the others and a Muslim may choose any one that he or she finds agreeable.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Schools_of_Islamic_theology" title="Schools of Islamic theology">Sunni schools of theology</a> encompass <a href="/wiki/Ash%CA%BFari" title="AshÊ¿ari">AshÊ¿arirism</a> founded by <a href="/wiki/Al-Ash%CA%BFar%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-AshÊ¿arÄ«">Al-AshÊ¿arÄ«</a> (c. 874â936), <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidi</a> by <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansur_al-Maturidi" title="Abu Mansur al-Maturidi">Abu Mansur al-Maturidi</a> (853â944 CE) and <a href="/wiki/Traditionalist_theology_(Islam)" title="Traditionalist theology (Islam)">Traditionalist theology</a> under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a> (780â855 CE). Traditionalist theology is characterized by its adherence to a literal understanding of the Quran and the Sunnah, the belief in the Quran to be uncreated and eternal, and opposes reason (<a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">kalam</a>) in religious and ethical matters.<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, Maturidism asserts, scripture is not needed for basic ethics and that <i>good</i> and <i>evil</i> can be understood by reason alone.<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup> Maturidi's doctrine, based on Hanafi-law, asserted  man's capacity and will alongside the supremacy of God in man's acts, providing a doctrinal framework for more flexibility, adaptability and syncretism. Maturidism especially flourished in <a href="/wiki/Central-Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Central-Asia">Central-Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, people would rely on revelation, because reason alone could not grasp the whole truth. Asharism holds, ethics can just derive from divine revelation, but not from human reason. However, Asharism accepts reason in regard of exegetical matters and combined <a href="/wiki/Mu%CA%BFtazila" title="MuÊ¿tazila">MuÊ¿tazila</a> approaches with traditionalistic ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Abd_al-Wahhab" title="Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab">Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab</a> led a Salafi movement, referred by outsiders as <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabism</a>, in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>. Originally shaped by <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbalism</a>, many modern followers departed from any of the established four schools of law Hanafi, Shafi, Maliki, and Hanbali.<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Hadith" title="Ahl al-Hadith">Ahl al-Hadith</a> is a movement that deemphasized sources of jurisprudence outside the quran and hadith, such as informed opinion (ra'y).The <a href="/wiki/Deobandi_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Deobandi movement">Deobandi movement</a> is a reformist movement originating in South Asia, influenced by the Wahhabi movement.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Nur_movement" title="Nur movement">Nurcu</a></i> is a Sunni movement based on the writings of <a href="/wiki/Said_Nursi" class="mw-redirect" title="Said Nursi">Said Nursi</a> (1877 â 1960) founded at the beginning of the twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Svante_E._Cornell_p._283_348-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svante_E._Cornell_p._283-348">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> His philosophy is based on Hanafi law and further incorporates elements of Sufism.<sup id="cite_ref-Svante_E._Cornell_p._283_348-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svante_E._Cornell_p._283-348">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> He emphasized the importance of salvation in both life and <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a> through education and freedom, the synthesis of Islam and science and democracy as the best form governance within the rule of law.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> Through faith by inquiry instead of faith by imitation, Muslims would reject philosophies such as <a href="/wiki/Positivism" title="Positivism">positivism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a> emerging from the Western world of his time.<sup id="cite_ref-Svante_E._Cornell_p._283_348-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svante_E._Cornell_p._283-348">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> His notion of <i>sharia</i> is twofold: On one hand, <i>sharia</i> applies to the voluntary actions of human beings. On the other hand, <i>sharia</i> denotes the set of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_law" title="Scientific law">laws of nature</a>, but both ultimately derive from one source, which is God.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup> His works on the Quran <a href="/wiki/Risale-i_Nur" title="Risale-i Nur">Risale-i Nur</a> was translated into almost all languages of Central Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> From <i>Nurcu</i> other movements such as the <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BClen_movement" title="GÃ¼len movement">GÃ¼len movement</a> derived.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shia">Shia</span></h3>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks collapsible collapsed" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;font-size:65%;"><span style="padding-left:2.25em;">&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/File:Basmala.svg" class="image" title="Bismillahir Rahmanir Rahim"><img alt="Bismillahir Rahmanir Rahim" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basmala.svg/195px-Basmala.svg.png" decoding="async" width="195" height="38" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basmala.svg/293px-Basmala.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Basmala.svg/390px-Basmala.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="608" data-file-height="119" /></a>

</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.1em;font-weight:bold;line-height:1.5em;">
<a href="/wiki/File:Ghadir_logo.png" class="image"><img alt="Ghadir logo.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Ghadir_logo.png/16px-Ghadir_logo.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Ghadir_logo.png/24px-Ghadir_logo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Ghadir_logo.png/32px-Ghadir_logo.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="720" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Shia_Islam" title="Portal:Shia Islam">Shia Islam&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Shia_Islam" title="Template:Shia Islam"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Shia_Islam" title="Template talk:Shia Islam"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Shia_Islam&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg/220px-Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg/330px-Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg/440px-Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2464" data-file-height="1632" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kerbela_Hussein_Moschee.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Imam_Husayn_Shrine" title="Imam Husayn Shrine">Imam Hussein Shrine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Karbala" title="Karbala">Karbala</a>, Iraq is a holy site for Shia Muslims.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Safavid_conversion_of_Iran_to_Shia_Islam" title="Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam">Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam</a></div>
<p>The Shia constitute 10â20% of Islam and are its second-largest branch.<sup id="cite_ref-Shia_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shia-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the Sunnis believe that a Caliph should be elected by the community, Shia's believe that Muhammad appointed his son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a>, as his successor and only certain descendants of Ali could be Imams. As a result, they believe that <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a> was the first <i>Imam</i> (leader), rejecting the legitimacy of the previous Muslim caliphs <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_al-Affan" class="mw-redirect" title="Uthman ibn al-Affan">Uthman ibn al-Affan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_al-Khattab" class="mw-redirect" title="Umar ibn al-Khattab">Umar ibn al-Khattab</a>. Other points of contention include certain practices viewed as <a href="/wiki/Bid%E2%80%98ah" title="Bidâah">innovating</a> the religion, such as the mourning practice of <a href="/wiki/Tatbir" title="Tatbir">tatbir</a>, and the cursing of figures revered by Sunnis. However, <a href="/wiki/Jafar_al-Sadiq" class="mw-redirect" title="Jafar al-Sadiq">Jafar al-Sadiq</a> himself disapproved of people who disapproved of his great grand father <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Ali" title="Zayd ibn Ali">Zayd ibn Ali</a> revered Abu Bakr and <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TabarÄ«,_Carole_Hillenbrand_1989,_p37_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TabarÄ«,_Carole_Hillenbrand_1989,_p37-352">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Religion_Vol_1987,_p243_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Religion_Vol_1987,_p243-353">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup> More recently, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Ayatollah" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Ayatollah">Grand Ayatollah</a> <a href="/wiki/Ali_Khamenei" title="Ali Khamenei">Ali Khamenei</a><sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> and Grand Ayatollah <a href="/wiki/Ali_al-Sistani" title="Ali al-Sistani">Ali al-Sistani</a><sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup> condemned the practice.
</p><p>Shia Islam has several branches, the most prominent being the <a href="/wiki/Twelvers" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelvers">Twelvers</a> (the largest branch), <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaidis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ismaili" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismaili">Ismailis</a>. Different branches accept different descendants of Ali as Imams. After the death of Imam <a href="/wiki/Jafar_al-Sadiq" class="mw-redirect" title="Jafar al-Sadiq">Jafar al-Sadiq</a> who is considered the sixth Imam by the <a href="/wiki/Twelvers" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelvers">Twelvers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ismaili" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismaili">Ismaili</a>'s, the Ismailis recognized his son Isma'il ibn Jafar as his successor whereas the Twelver Shia's (Ithna Asheri) followed his other son <a href="/wiki/Musa_al-Kadhim" title="Musa al-Kadhim">Musa al-Kadhim</a> as the seventh Imam. The <a href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaydis</a> consider <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Ali" title="Zayd ibn Ali">Zayd ibn Ali</a>, the uncle of Imam <a href="/wiki/Jafar_al-Sadiq" class="mw-redirect" title="Jafar al-Sadiq">Jafar al-Sadiq</a>, as their fifth Imam, and follow a different line of succession after him. Other smaller groups include the <a href="/wiki/Mustaali" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustaali">Bohra</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Alawites" title="Alawites">Alawites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alevi" class="mw-redirect" title="Alevi">Alevi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> Some Shia branches label other Shia branches that do not agree with their doctrine as <a href="/wiki/Ghulat" title="Ghulat">Ghulat</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_denominations">Other denominations</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a> is an Islamic reform movement (with Sunni roots) founded by <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Ghulam_Ahmad" title="Mirza Ghulam Ahmad">Mirza Ghulam Ahmad</a><sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup> that began in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> in 1889 and is practiced by 10 to 20 million<sup id="cite_ref-ahmadi_358-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ahmadi-358">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> Muslims around the world. Ahmad claimed to have fulfilled the prophecies concerning the arrival of the 'Imam Mahdi' and the 'Promised Messiah'.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alevism" title="Alevism">Bektashi Alevism</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heterodoxy" title="Heterodoxy">heterodox</a> local Islamic tradition, whose adherents follow the mystical (<a href="/wiki/Batin_(Islam)" title="Batin (Islam)">bÄá¹­enÄ«</a>) teachings of Ali and <a href="/wiki/Haji_Bektash_Veli" title="Haji Bektash Veli">Haji Bektash Veli</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup> Alevism incorporates Turkish beliefs present during the 14th century,<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Tengrism" title="Tengrism">Shamanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">Animism</a>, mixed with Shias and Sufi beliefs, adopted by some Turkish tribes.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Ibadi" title="Ibadi">Ibadi</a> is a sect that dates back to the early days of Islam and is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Kharijite" class="mw-redirect" title="Kharijite">Kharijite</a> and is practiced by 1.45&#160;million Muslims around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup> Unlike most Kharijite groups, Ibadism does not regard sinful Muslims as unbelievers.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mahdavia" title="Mahdavia">Mahdavia</a> is an Islamic sect that believes in a 15th-century Mahdi, Muhammad Jaunpuri</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Quran_alone" class="mw-redirect" title="Quran alone">Quranists</a> are Muslims who generally reject the Hadith.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-denominational_Muslims">Non-denominational Muslims</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-denominational_Muslim" title="Non-denominational Muslim">Non-denominational Muslim</a></div>
<p>Non-denominational Muslims is an <a href="/wiki/Umbrella_term" class="mw-redirect" title="Umbrella term">umbrella term</a> that has been used for and by <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> who do not belong to or do not self-identify with a specific <a href="/wiki/Islamic_denomination" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic denomination">Islamic denomination</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kirkham_363-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirkham-363">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pollack_364-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pollack-364">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> Prominent figures who refused to identify with a particular Islamic denomination have included <a href="/wiki/Jamal_ad-Din_al-Afghani" class="mw-redirect" title="Jamal ad-Din al-Afghani">Jamal ad-Din al-Afghani</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Iqbal" title="Muhammad Iqbal">Muhammad Iqbal</a><sup id="cite_ref-Jones2011_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2011-366">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Jinnah" title="Muhammad Ali Jinnah">Muhammad Ali Jinnah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> Recent surveys report that large proportions of Muslims in some parts of the world self-identify as "just Muslim", although there is little published analysis available regarding the motivations underlying this response.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> reports that respondents self-identifying as "just Muslim" make up a majority of Muslims in seven countries (and a plurality in three others), with the highest proportion in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> at 74%. At least one in five Muslims in at least 22 countries self-identify in this way.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Derived_religions">Derived religions</span></h3>
<p>Some movements, such as the <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berghouata" class="mw-redirect" title="Berghouata">Berghouata</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ha-Mim" title="Ha-Mim">Ha-Mim</a>, either emerged from Islam or came to share certain beliefs with Islam and whether each is a separate religion or a sect of Islam is sometimes controversial. <a href="/wiki/Yazd%C3%A2nism" title="YazdÃ¢nism">YazdÃ¢nism</a> is seen as a blend of local Kurdish beliefs and Islamic Sufi doctrine introduced to <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan" title="Kurdistan">Kurdistan</a> by <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Adi_ibn_Musafir" title="Sheikh Adi ibn Musafir">Sheikh Adi ibn Musafir</a> in the 12th century. <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1bism" title="BÃ¡bism">BÃ¡bism</a> stems from Twelver Shia passed through <a href="/wiki/Siyyid_%27Ali_Muhammad_i-Shirazi_al-Bab" class="mw-redirect" title="Siyyid &#39;Ali Muhammad i-Shirazi al-Bab">Siyyid 'Ali Muhammad i-Shirazi al-Bab</a> while one of his followers Mirza Husayn 'Ali Nuri <a href="/wiki/Baha%27u%27llah" class="mw-redirect" title="Baha&#39;u&#39;llah">Baha'u'llah</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Bahai_Faith" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahai Faith">Bahai Faith</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, founded by <a href="/wiki/Guru_Nanak" title="Guru Nanak">Guru Nanak</a> in late-fifteenth-century <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a>, incorporates aspects of both Islam and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>. African American Muslim movements include the <a href="/wiki/Nation_of_Islam" title="Nation of Islam">Nation of Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Five-Percent_Nation" title="Five-Percent Nation">Five-Percent Nation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moorish_scientists" class="mw-redirect" title="Moorish scientists">Moorish scientists</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Ummah</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">Islam by country</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg/275px-Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg.png" decoding="async" width="275" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg/413px-Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg/550px-Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="443" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Islam_percent_population_in_each_nation_World_Map_Muslim_data_by_Pew_Research.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World Muslim population by percentage (<a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, 2014).</div></div></div>
<p>A comprehensive 2019 demographic study of 232 countries and territories reported that 24.4% of the global population, or 1.9&#160;billion people, are Muslims. Of those, it is estimated that over 75â90% are <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> and 10â20% are <a href="/wiki/Shia" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia">Shia</a><sup id="cite_ref-mgmpPRC_372-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mgmpPRC-372">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_336-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-336">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-373" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-373">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup> with a small minority belonging to other sects. Approximately 57 countries are <a href="/wiki/List_of_Muslim_majority_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Muslim majority countries">Muslim-majority</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-374" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-374">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> account for around 20% of all Muslims worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-375" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-375">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup> The number of Muslims worldwide increased from 200 million in 1900 to 551&#160;million in 1970,<sup id="cite_ref-376" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-376">&#91;374&#93;</a></sup> and tripled to 1.9&#160;billion by 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-pew2015_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew2015-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority of Muslims live in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-377" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-377">&#91;375&#93;</a></sup> Approximately 62% of the world's Muslims live in Asia, with over 683&#160;million adherents in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USN&amp;WR_378-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN&amp;WR-378">&#91;376&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-379" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-379">&#91;377&#93;</a></sup> In the Middle East, non-Arab countries such as <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> are the largest Muslim-majority countries; in Africa, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> have the most populous Muslim communities.
</p><p>Most estimates indicate that the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> has approximately 20 to 30 million Muslims (1.5% to 2% of the population).<sup id="cite_ref-380" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-380">&#91;378&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-381" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-381">&#91;379&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-382" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-382">&#91;380&#93;</a></sup> However, data provided by the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_State_University" title="San Diego State University">San Diego State University</a>'s International Population Center to <i><a href="/wiki/U.S._News_%26_World_Report" title="U.S. News &amp; World Report">U.S. News &amp; World Report</a></i> suggests that China has 65.3&#160;million Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-383" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-383">&#91;381&#93;</a></sup> Islam is the second largest religion after <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> in many European countries,<sup id="cite_ref-384" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-384">&#91;382&#93;</a></sup> and is slowly catching up to that status in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, with between 2,454,000, according to <a href="/wiki/Pew_Forum" class="mw-redirect" title="Pew Forum">Pew Forum</a>, and approximately 7 million Muslims, according to the <a href="/wiki/Council_on_American%E2%80%93Islamic_Relations" title="Council on AmericanâIslamic Relations">Council on AmericanâIslamic Relations (CAIR)</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mgmpPRC_372-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mgmpPRC-372">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-385" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-385">&#91;383&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jakarta_Skyline_Part_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Jakarta_Skyline_Part_2.jpg/275px-Jakarta_Skyline_Part_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="275" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Jakarta_Skyline_Part_2.jpg/413px-Jakarta_Skyline_Part_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Jakarta_Skyline_Part_2.jpg/550px-Jakarta_Skyline_Part_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="906" data-file-height="450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jakarta_Skyline_Part_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Skyline" title="Skyline">Skyline</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, capital of <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, the most populous Muslim-majority country.</div></div></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, Islam is set to equal Christianity worldwide in number of adherents by the year 2050. Islam is set to grow faster than any other major world religion, reaching a total number of 2.76&#160;billion (an increase of 73%). Causes of this trend involve high <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">fertility rates</a> as a factor, with <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> having a rate of 3.1 compared to the world average of 2.5, and the minimum replacement level for a population at 2.1. Another factor is also due to fact that Islam has the highest number of adherents under the age of 15 (34% of the total religion) of any major religion, compared with Christianity's 27%. 60% of Muslims are between the ages of 16 and 59, while only 7% are aged 60+ (the smallest percentage of any major religion). Countries such as <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Macedonia" title="North Macedonia">North Macedonia</a> are expected to have Muslim majorities by 2050. In India, the Muslim population will be larger than any other country. Europe's non-Muslim population is set to decline as opposed to their Muslim population which is set to grow to 10% of Europe's total.<sup id="cite_ref-pew2015_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew2015-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Growth rates of <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Europe" title="Islam in Europe">Islam in Europe</a> was due primarily to immigration and higher birth rates of Muslims in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-386" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-386">&#91;384&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_culture" title="Islamic culture">Islamic culture</a></div>
<p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Islamic_culture" title="Islamic culture">Islamic culture</a>" could be used to mean aspects of culture that pertain to the religion, such as <a href="/wiki/Festivals" class="mw-redirect" title="Festivals">festivals</a> and dress code. It is also controversially used to denote the cultural aspects of traditionally <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> people.<sup id="cite_ref-387" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-387">&#91;385&#93;</a></sup> Finally, "Islamic civilization" may also refer to the aspects of the synthesized culture of the early Caliphates, including that of non-Muslims,<sup id="cite_ref-388" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-388">&#91;386&#93;</a></sup> sometimes referred to as "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Islamicate" class="extiw" title="wikt:Islamicate">Islamicate</a>".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a></div>
<p>Perhaps the most important expression of Islamic architecture is that of the mosque.<sup id="cite_ref-389" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-389">&#91;387&#93;</a></sup> Varying cultures have an effect on mosque architecture. For example, North African and Spanish Islamic architecture such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan" title="Great Mosque of Kairouan">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> contain <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(geology)" title="Porphyry (geology)">porphyry</a> columns from Roman and Byzantine buildings,<sup id="cite_ref-390" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-390">&#91;388&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Mosques_in_Indonesia" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosques in Indonesia">mosques in Indonesia</a> often have multi-tiered roofs from local <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Javanese</a> styles.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic art</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic art</a> encompasses the <a href="/wiki/Visual_arts" title="Visual arts">visual arts</a> produced from the 7th century onwards by people (not necessarily <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a>) who lived within the territory that was inhabited by Muslim populations.<sup id="cite_ref-391" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-391">&#91;389&#93;</a></sup> It includes fields as varied as architecture, <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a>, painting, and <a href="/wiki/Ceramics_(art)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceramics (art)">ceramics</a>, among others.
</p><p>While not condemned in the Quran, making images of human beings and animals is frowned on in many Islamic cultures and connected with <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Islam" title="Aniconism in Islam">laws against idolatry</a> common to all Abrahamic religions, as 'Abdullaah ibn Mas'ood reported that Muhammad said, "Those who will be most severely punished by Allah on the Day of Resurrection will be the image-makers" (reported by al-Bukhaari, see al-Fath, 10/382). However this rule has been interpreted in different ways by different scholars and in different historical periods, and there are examples of paintings of both animals and humans in Mughal, Persian and Turkish art. The existence of this <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Islam" title="Aniconism in Islam">aversion to creating images of animate beings</a> has been used to explain the prevalence of calligraphy, tessellation and pattern as key aspects of Islamic artistic culture.<sup id="cite_ref-392" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-392">&#91;390&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 275px"><div style="width: 275px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_music" title="Islamic music">Islamic music</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry">Poetry</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_poetry" title="Islamic poetry">Islamic poetry</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calendar">Calendar</span></h3>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a></th></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em">
Months</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;text-align:left">
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Muharram" title="Muharram">Muharram</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Safar" title="Safar">Safar</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rabi%27_al-awwal" title="Rabi&#39; al-awwal">Rabi' al-awwal</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Jumada_al-awwal" title="Jumada al-awwal">Jumada al-awwal</a></li>

</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Muslimmonths" title="Template:Muslimmonths"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Muslimmonths" title="Template talk:Muslimmonths"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Muslimmonths&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lunar_libration_with_phase_Oct_2007.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Lunar_libration_with_phase_Oct_2007.gif/220px-Lunar_libration_with_phase_Oct_2007.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Lunar_libration_with_phase_Oct_2007.gif/330px-Lunar_libration_with_phase_Oct_2007.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Lunar_libration_with_phase_Oct_2007.gif/440px-Lunar_libration_with_phase_Oct_2007.gif 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="642" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lunar_libration_with_phase_Oct_2007.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The phases of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> form the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a></div></div></div>
<p>The formal beginning of the Muslim era was chosen, reportedly by <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Caliph Umar</a>, to be the <a href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hijra (Islam)">Hijra</a> in 622 CE, which was an important turning point in Muhammad's fortunes. It is a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar calendar</a> with days lasting from sunset to sunset.<sup id="cite_ref-393" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-393">&#91;391&#93;</a></sup> Islamic <a href="/wiki/Muslim_holidays" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim holidays">holy days</a> fall on fixed dates of the lunar calendar, which means that they occur in <a href="/wiki/Seasons" class="mw-redirect" title="Seasons">different seasons</a> in different years in the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>. The most important Islamic festivals are <i><a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Fitr" title="Eid al-Fitr">Eid al-Fitr</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">Ø¹ÙØ¯ Ø§ÙÙØ·Ø±</span>&#8206;) on the 1st of <i><a href="/wiki/Shawwal" title="Shawwal">Shawwal</a></i>, marking the end of the fasting month <i>Ramadan</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Adha" title="Eid al-Adha">Eid al-Adha</a></i> (<span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">Ø¹ÙØ¯ Ø§ÙØ£Ø¶Ø­Ù</span>) on the 10th of <i><a href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah" title="Dhu al-Hijjah">Dhu al-Hijjah</a></i>, coinciding with the end of the Hajj pilgrimage.<sup id="cite_ref-394" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-394">&#91;392&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Islam" title="Criticism of Islam">Criticism of Islam</a></div> 
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Damascus_(arabic_icon).gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/John_Damascus_%28arabic_icon%29.gif/200px-John_Damascus_%28arabic_icon%29.gif" decoding="async" width="200" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/John_Damascus_%28arabic_icon%29.gif/300px-John_Damascus_%28arabic_icon%29.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/John_Damascus_%28arabic_icon%29.gif 2x" data-file-width="364" data-file-height="574" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Damascus_(arabic_icon).gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_of_Damascus" title="John of Damascus">John of Damascus</a> viewed Islamic doctrines as nothing more than a hodgepodge culled from the Bible.<sup id="cite_ref-395" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-395">&#91;393&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Criticism of Islam has existed since Islam's formative stages. Early criticism came from Christian authors, many of whom viewed Islam as a Christian <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> or a form of idolatry and often explained it in apocalyptic terms.<sup id="cite_ref-396" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-396">&#91;394&#93;</a></sup> Later there appeared criticism from the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> itself, and also from <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> writers and from ecclesiastical Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-WarraqPoetry_397-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WarraqPoetry-397">&#91;395&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ibn_Kammuna_398-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ibn_Kammuna-398">&#91;396&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oussani_399-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oussani-399">&#91;397&#93;</a></sup> Issues relating to the authenticity and morality of the Quran, the Islamic holy book, are also discussed by critics.<sup id="cite_ref-BibleInQuran_400-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BibleInQuran-400">&#91;398&#93;</a></sup> Islamic salvation optimism and its carnality was criticized by Christian writers. Islam's sensual descriptions of paradise led many Christians to conclude that Islam was not a spiritual religion. Although sensual pleasure was also present in early Christianity, as seen in the writings of <a href="/wiki/Irenaeus" title="Irenaeus">Irenaeus</a>, the doctrines of the former <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaean</a> <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> led to broad repudiation of bodily pleasure in both life and the afterlife. <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Sahl_Rabban_al-Tabari" title="Ali ibn Sahl Rabban al-Tabari">Ali ibn Sahl Rabban al-Tabari</a> defended the Quranic description of paradise by asserting that the Bible also implies such ideas, such as drinking wine in <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel of Matthew</a>.
</p><p>Defamatory images of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Christian_views_on_Muhammad" title="Medieval Christian views on Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, derived from early 7th century depictions of <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Church#Byzantine_period" title="History of the Eastern Orthodox Church">Byzantine Church</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceH_401-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceH-401">&#91;399&#93;</a></sup> appear in the 14th-century <a href="/wiki/Epic_poem" class="mw-redirect" title="Epic poem">epic poem</a> <a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a> by <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante Alighieri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_402-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-402">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup>  Here, Muhammad appears in the eighth circle of hell, along with Ali. Dante does not blame Islam as a whole, but accuses Muhammad of <a href="/wiki/Schism" title="Schism">schism</a>, by establishing another religion after Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_402-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-402">&#91;400&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">the events of September 11, 2001</a>, Islam has <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Islam" title="Criticism of Islam">faced criticism</a> over its scriptures and teachings being a significant <a href="/wiki/Islamic_terrorism" title="Islamic terrorism">source of terrorism</a> <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">and terrorist ideology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-403" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-403">&#91;401&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-404" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-404">&#91;402&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other criticisms focus on the question of human rights in modern Muslim-majority countries, and the treatment of women in Islamic law and practice.<sup id="cite_ref-women_405-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-women-405">&#91;403&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IslamInEurope_406-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IslamInEurope-406">&#91;404&#93;</a></sup> In wake of the recent <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">multiculturalism</a> trend, Islam's influence on the ability of Muslim immigrants in the West to assimilate has been <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_multiculturalism" title="Criticism of multiculturalism">criticized</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Modood_407-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Modood-407">&#91;405&#93;</a></sup> Both in his public and personal life, others objected the morality of Muhammad, therefore also the sunnah as a role model.<sup id="cite_ref-Oussani_399-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oussani-399">&#91;397&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WarraqQuest_408-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WarraqQuest-408">&#91;406&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-210">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Watt. <i>Muhammad at Medina</i>. pp. 227â228 Watt argues that the initial agreement came about shortly after the hijra and that the document was amended at a later dateâspecifically after the battle of Badr (AH [anno hijra] 2, = AD 624). Serjeant argues that the constitution is in fact 8 different treaties which can be dated according to events as they transpired in Medina, with the first treaty written shortly after Muhammad's arrival. R.B. Serjeant. "The Sunnah JÃ¢mi'ah, Pacts with the Yathrib Jews, and the TahrÃ®m of Yathrib: Analysis and Translation of the Documents Comprised in the so-called 'Constitution of Medina'." in <i>The Life of Muhammad: The Formation of the Classical Islamic World</i>: Volume iv. Ed. Uri Rubin. Brookfield: Ashgate, 1998, p. 151 and see same article in BSOAS 41 (1978): 18 ff. See also Caetani. <i>Annali dell'Islam, Volume I</i>. Milano: Hoepli, 1905, p. 393. Julius Wellhausen. <i>Skizzen und Vorabeiten</i>, IV, Berlin: Reimer, 1889, p 82f who argue that the document is a single treaty agreed upon shortly after the hijra. Wellhausen argues that it belongs to the first year of Muhammad's residence in Medina, before the battle of Badr in 2/624. Even Moshe Gil a skeptic of Islamic history argues that it was written within five months of Muhammad's arrival in Medina. Moshe Gil. "The Constitution of Medina: A Reconsideration." <i>Israel Oriental Studies</i> 4 (1974): p. 45.</span>

<li id="cite_note-392"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-392">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Salim Ayduz; Ibrahim Kalin; Caner Dagli (2014). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=or-6BwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA263&amp;lpg=PA263&amp;dq=islamic+art+idolatry+geometry#v=onepage&amp;q=islamic%20art%20idolatry%20geometry"><i>The Oxford Encyclopedia of Philosophy, Science, and Technology in Islam</i></a>. Oxford University Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780199812578" title="Special:BookSources/9780199812578"><bdi>9780199812578</bdi></a>. <q>Figural representation is virtually unused in Islamic art because of Islam's strong antagonism of idolatry. It was important for Muslim scholars and artists to find a style of art that represented the Islamic ideals of unity (<i>tawhid</i>) and order without figural representation.  Geometric patterns perfectly suited this goal.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy%2C+Science%2C+and+Technology+in+Islam&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=9780199812578&amp;rft.au=Salim+Ayduz&amp;rft.au=Ibrahim+Kalin&amp;rft.au=Caner+Dagli&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3Dor-6BwAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA263%26lpg%3DPA263%26dq%3Dislamic%2Bart%2Bidolatry%2Bgeometry%23v%3Donepage%26q%3Dislamic%2520art%2520idolatry%2520geometry&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIslam" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
